\includegraphics{05_Time_Series/01__Soybean_Yield_Prediction/notebooks/Crop_yield_estimationR4_files/figure-pdf/cell-22-output-2.png}

\subsection{3.3 Phenological (Temporal) Importance
Analysis}\label{phenological-temporal-importance-analysis}

Understanding \textbf{when} during the growing season environmental
conditions most strongly influence final yield is crucial for:

\textbf{Scientific Understanding:} - \textbf{Phenological Insights}:
Validate known critical periods (flowering, grain filling) -
\textbf{Climate Sensitivity}: Understand when crops are most vulnerable
to weather stress - \textbf{Yield Formation}: Quantify relative
importance of different developmental stages

\textbf{Model Interpretation}: - \textbf{Temporal Attention}: Understand
which time periods the model focuses on - \textbf{Seasonal Patterns}:
Identify consistent vs.~variable importance across the season -
\textbf{Data Collection Priority}: Guide decisions about temporal
resolution of monitoring

\textbf{Methodology}: Instead of permuting individual features, we
permute \textbf{entire time steps}, disrupting the temporal
relationships while preserving the feature distributions at each time
point.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\KeywordTok{def}\NormalTok{ timestep\_importance(model, X, y, metric, n\_repeats}\OperatorTok{=}\DecValTok{10}\NormalTok{):}
    \CommentTok{"""}
\CommentTok{    Calculate importance scores for each time step using permutation importance.}

\CommentTok{    Parameters:}
\CommentTok{    {-}{-}{-}{-}{-}{-}{-}{-}{-}{-}{-}}
\CommentTok{    model : keras.Model}
\CommentTok{        Trained model}
\CommentTok{    X : numpy.ndarray}
\CommentTok{        Input data of shape (samples, timesteps, features)}
\CommentTok{    y : numpy.ndarray}
\CommentTok{        Target values}
\CommentTok{    metric : function}
\CommentTok{        Scoring metric (e.g., r2\_score)}
\CommentTok{    n\_repeats : int}
\CommentTok{        Number of times to repeat the permutation}

\CommentTok{    Returns:}
\CommentTok{    {-}{-}{-}{-}{-}{-}{-}{-}}
\CommentTok{    importances : list}
\CommentTok{        Importance score for each time step}
\CommentTok{    importances\_std : list}
\CommentTok{        Standard deviation of importance scores for each time step}
\CommentTok{    timesteps : list}
\CommentTok{        List of time step indices}
\CommentTok{    """}

    \CommentTok{\# Set random seed for reproducibility}
\NormalTok{    np.random.seed(}\DecValTok{32}\NormalTok{)}

\NormalTok{    baseline\_score }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ metric(y, model.predict(X)[:, }\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{])}
\NormalTok{    importances }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ []}
\NormalTok{    importances\_std }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ []}
\NormalTok{    timesteps }\OperatorTok{=} \BuiltInTok{list}\NormalTok{(}\BuiltInTok{range}\NormalTok{(X.shape[}\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{]))  }\CommentTok{\# Get list of timestep indices}

    \CommentTok{\# For each timestep}
    \ControlFlowTok{for}\NormalTok{ timestep\_idx }\KeywordTok{in} \BuiltInTok{range}\NormalTok{(X.shape[}\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{]):}
\NormalTok{        scores }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ []}
        \ControlFlowTok{for}\NormalTok{ \_ }\KeywordTok{in} \BuiltInTok{range}\NormalTok{(n\_repeats):}
\NormalTok{            X\_permuted }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ X.copy()}
            \CommentTok{\# Permute all features at this timestep}
\NormalTok{            permuted\_values }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ np.random.permutation(X\_permuted[:, timestep\_idx, :])}
\NormalTok{            X\_permuted[:, timestep\_idx, :] }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ permuted\_values}
\NormalTok{            permuted\_score }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ metric(y, model.predict(X\_permuted)[:, }\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{])}
\NormalTok{            scores.append(baseline\_score }\OperatorTok{{-}}\NormalTok{ permuted\_score)}

\NormalTok{        mean\_score }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ np.mean(scores)}
\NormalTok{        std\_score }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ np.std(scores)}

        \ControlFlowTok{if}\NormalTok{ mean\_score }\OperatorTok{\textgreater{}} \DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{:}
\NormalTok{            importances.append(mean\_score)}
        \ControlFlowTok{else}\NormalTok{:}
\NormalTok{            importances.append(}\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{)}

\NormalTok{        importances\_std.append(std\_score)}

    \ControlFlowTok{return}\NormalTok{ importances, importances\_std, timesteps}

\KeywordTok{def}\NormalTok{ plot\_timestep\_importance(importances, importances\_std, timesteps, window\_size):}
    \CommentTok{"""}
\CommentTok{    Plot timestep importance scores with error bars.}

\CommentTok{    Parameters:}
\CommentTok{    {-}{-}{-}{-}{-}{-}{-}{-}{-}{-}{-}}
\CommentTok{    importances : list}
\CommentTok{        Importance scores}
\CommentTok{    importances\_std : list}
\CommentTok{        Standard deviation of importance scores}
\CommentTok{    timesteps : list}
\CommentTok{        Time step indices}
\CommentTok{    window\_size : int}
\CommentTok{        Size of the input window in days}
\CommentTok{    """}
\NormalTok{    plt.figure(figsize}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{(}\DecValTok{8}\OperatorTok{/}\FloatTok{1.5}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{6}\OperatorTok{/}\FloatTok{1.5}\NormalTok{))}

    \CommentTok{\# Convert timestep indices to days before prediction}
\NormalTok{    days\_before }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ [(window\_size }\OperatorTok{{-}}\NormalTok{ i) }\ControlFlowTok{for}\NormalTok{ i }\KeywordTok{in}\NormalTok{ timesteps]}

\NormalTok{    plt.bar(days\_before, importances, yerr}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{importances\_std,}
            \CommentTok{\# capsize=5, error\_kw=\{\textquotesingle{}elinewidth\textquotesingle{}: 2, \textquotesingle{}alpha\textquotesingle{}: 0.8\})}
\NormalTok{            capsize}\OperatorTok{=}\DecValTok{4}\NormalTok{, alpha}\OperatorTok{=}\FloatTok{0.8}\NormalTok{, color}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}cornflowerblue\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, ecolor}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}black\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{    plt.xlabel(}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}Phenological Stage\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{    plt.ylabel(}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}Importance Score (decrease in R²)\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{    plt.title(}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}Phenological Stage Importance Based on Permutation Test\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{    plt.grid(}\VariableTok{True}\NormalTok{, axis}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}y\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, linestyle}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}{-}{-}\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, alpha}\OperatorTok{=}\FloatTok{0.7}\NormalTok{)}

\NormalTok{    plt.tight\_layout()}
\NormalTok{    plt.show()}

\KeywordTok{def}\NormalTok{ analyze\_timestep\_importance(model, X, y, window\_size, metric}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{r2\_score, n\_repeats}\OperatorTok{=}\DecValTok{10}\NormalTok{):}
    \CommentTok{"""}
\CommentTok{    Analyze and visualize time step importance.}

\CommentTok{    Parameters:}
\CommentTok{    {-}{-}{-}{-}{-}{-}{-}{-}{-}{-}{-}}
\CommentTok{    model : keras.Model}
\CommentTok{        Trained model}
\CommentTok{    X : numpy.ndarray}
\CommentTok{        Input data}
\CommentTok{    y : numpy.ndarray}
\CommentTok{        Target values}
\CommentTok{    window\_size : int}
\CommentTok{        Size of the input window in days}
\CommentTok{    metric : function}
\CommentTok{        Scoring metric (default: r2\_score)}
\CommentTok{    n\_repeats : int}
\CommentTok{        Number of permutation repeats (default: 10)}
\CommentTok{    """}
    \BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"Calculating timestep importance scores..."}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{    importance\_scores, importance\_stds, timesteps }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ timestep\_importance(model, X, y, metric, n\_repeats)}

    \CommentTok{\# Print results sorted by importance}
\NormalTok{    results }\OperatorTok{=} \BuiltInTok{list}\NormalTok{(}\BuiltInTok{zip}\NormalTok{(timesteps, importance\_scores, importance\_stds))}
\NormalTok{    results.sort(key}\OperatorTok{=}\KeywordTok{lambda}\NormalTok{ x: x[}\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{], reverse}\OperatorTok{=}\VariableTok{True}\NormalTok{)}

    \BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"}\CharTok{\textbackslash{}n}\StringTok{Phenology Stage Importance Rankings:"}\NormalTok{)}
    \BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"Phenology stage| Importance Score | Std Dev"}\NormalTok{)}
    \BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"{-}"} \OperatorTok{*} \DecValTok{55}\NormalTok{)}
    \ControlFlowTok{for}\NormalTok{ timestep, score, std }\KeywordTok{in}\NormalTok{ results:}
\NormalTok{        days\_before }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ window\_size }\OperatorTok{{-}}\NormalTok{ timestep}
        \BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(}\SpecialStringTok{f"}\SpecialCharTok{\{}\NormalTok{days\_before}\SpecialCharTok{:\^{}19\}}\SpecialStringTok{ | }\SpecialCharTok{\{}\NormalTok{score}\SpecialCharTok{:\textgreater{}15.4f\}}\SpecialStringTok{ | }\SpecialCharTok{\{}\NormalTok{std}\SpecialCharTok{:\textgreater{}7.4f\}}\SpecialStringTok{"}\NormalTok{)}

    \CommentTok{\# Create visualization}
\NormalTok{    plot\_timestep\_importance(importance\_scores, importance\_stds, timesteps, window\_size)}

    \ControlFlowTok{return}\NormalTok{ importance\_scores, importance\_stds, timesteps}

\NormalTok{window\_size }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ X\_train.shape[}\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{]  }\CommentTok{\# Your input window size}
\NormalTok{importance\_scores, importance\_stds, timesteps }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ analyze\_timestep\_importance(model, X\_test, Y\_test, window\_size)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
Calculating timestep importance scores...

\includegraphics{05_Time_Series/01__Soybean_Yield_Prediction/notebooks/Crop_yield_estimationR4_files/figure-pdf/cell-23-output-2.png}

\subsection{3.4 Spatial-Temporal Visualization and Uncertainty
Mapping}\label{spatial-temporal-visualization-and-uncertainty-mapping}

This section provides geographic visualization of model predictions and
uncertainties, enabling:

\textbf{Spatial Pattern Analysis:} - \textbf{Regional Performance}: How
well does the model perform in different geographic areas? -
\textbf{Spatial Uncertainty Patterns}: Are there geographic regions
where the model is less confident? - \textbf{Agricultural Zone
Validation}: Do predictions align with known agricultural productivity
zones?

\textbf{Temporal Consistency:} - \textbf{Year-to-Year Stability}: How
consistent are predictions across different growing seasons? -
\textbf{Climate Impact Visualization}: Spatial patterns of model
response to variable weather - \textbf{Risk Assessment}: Geographic
identification of high-uncertainty areas for targeted monitoring

\textbf{Visualization Features:} - \textbf{Choropleth Maps}:
County-level prediction and uncertainty visualization -
\textbf{Consistent Color Scales}: Enable cross-year comparison -
\textbf{Uncertainty Quantification}: Visual representation of model
confidence

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\KeywordTok{def}\NormalTok{ get\_overall\_ranges(df\_all, years\_range):}
    \CommentTok{"""Calculate min/max values for yield and uncertainty across all years."""}
\NormalTok{    all\_predictions }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ []}
\NormalTok{    all\_uncertainties }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ []}

    \ControlFlowTok{for}\NormalTok{ year }\KeywordTok{in}\NormalTok{ years\_range:}
\NormalTok{        X\_test }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ np.load(save\_path }\OperatorTok{+} \BuiltInTok{str}\NormalTok{(year) }\OperatorTok{+} \StringTok{\textquotesingle{}\_X.npy\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{        X\_test }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ normalize\_data(X\_test, X\_mean, X\_std)}
\NormalTok{        predictions }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ model.predict(X\_test)}
\NormalTok{        all\_predictions.extend(predictions[:, }\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{])}
\NormalTok{        all\_uncertainties.extend(predictions[:, }\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{])}

    \ControlFlowTok{return}\NormalTok{ \{}
        \StringTok{\textquotesingle{}yield\_min\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{: np.}\BuiltInTok{min}\NormalTok{(all\_predictions),}
        \StringTok{\textquotesingle{}yield\_max\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{: np.}\BuiltInTok{max}\NormalTok{(all\_predictions),}
        \StringTok{\textquotesingle{}uncertainty\_min\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{: np.}\BuiltInTok{min}\NormalTok{(all\_uncertainties),}
        \StringTok{\textquotesingle{}uncertainty\_max\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{: np.}\BuiltInTok{max}\NormalTok{(all\_uncertainties)}
\NormalTok{    \}}

\KeywordTok{def}\NormalTok{ plot\_yield\_prediction(counties, year, value\_ranges, figsize}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{(}\DecValTok{8}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{6}\NormalTok{), show\_coords}\OperatorTok{=}\VariableTok{True}\NormalTok{):}
    \CommentTok{"""}
\CommentTok{    Create yield prediction map with less dense ticks}
\CommentTok{    """}
\NormalTok{    yield\_colors }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ [}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}\#f7fcf0\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}\#e0f3db\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}\#ccebc5\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}\#a8ddb5\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}\#7bccc4\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{,}
                   \StringTok{\textquotesingle{}\#4eb3d3\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}\#2b8cbe\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}\#0868ac\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}\#084081\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{]}
\NormalTok{    yield\_cmap }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ LinearSegmentedColormap.from\_list(}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}yield\_professional\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, yield\_colors)}

\NormalTok{    fig, ax }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ plt.subplots(}\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{, figsize}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{figsize)}

    \CommentTok{\# Plot counties}
\NormalTok{    counties.boundary.plot(ax}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ax, linewidth}\OperatorTok{=}\FloatTok{0.2}\NormalTok{, color}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}\#666666\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, alpha}\OperatorTok{=}\FloatTok{0.8}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{    counties.plot(column}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}Predicted\_Yield\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{,}
\NormalTok{                 ax}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ax,}
\NormalTok{                 cmap}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{yield\_cmap,}
\NormalTok{                 vmin}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{value\_ranges[}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}yield\_min\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{],}
\NormalTok{                 vmax}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{value\_ranges[}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}yield\_max\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{],}
\NormalTok{                 edgecolor}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}none\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{)}

    \ControlFlowTok{if}\NormalTok{ show\_coords:}
\NormalTok{        bounds }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ counties.total\_bounds}
\NormalTok{        ax.set\_xlim(bounds[}\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{], bounds[}\DecValTok{2}\NormalTok{])}
\NormalTok{        ax.set\_ylim(bounds[}\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{], bounds[}\DecValTok{3}\NormalTok{])}

        \CommentTok{\# Create less dense coordinate ticks}
\NormalTok{        lon\_range }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ bounds[}\DecValTok{2}\NormalTok{] }\OperatorTok{{-}}\NormalTok{ bounds[}\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{]}
\NormalTok{        lat\_range }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ bounds[}\DecValTok{3}\NormalTok{] }\OperatorTok{{-}}\NormalTok{ bounds[}\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{]}

        \CommentTok{\# Increase spacing for less dense ticks}
        \ControlFlowTok{if}\NormalTok{ lon\_range }\OperatorTok{\textgreater{}} \DecValTok{20}\NormalTok{:}
\NormalTok{            lon\_step }\OperatorTok{=} \DecValTok{5}    \CommentTok{\# Changed from 2 to 5}
\NormalTok{            lat\_step }\OperatorTok{=} \DecValTok{2}    \CommentTok{\# Changed from 1 to 2}
        \ControlFlowTok{elif}\NormalTok{ lon\_range }\OperatorTok{\textgreater{}} \DecValTok{10}\NormalTok{:}
\NormalTok{            lon\_step }\OperatorTok{=} \DecValTok{3}    \CommentTok{\# For medium areas}
\NormalTok{            lat\_step }\OperatorTok{=} \DecValTok{2}
        \ControlFlowTok{else}\NormalTok{:}
\NormalTok{            lon\_step }\OperatorTok{=} \DecValTok{2}    \CommentTok{\# For smaller areas}
\NormalTok{            lat\_step }\OperatorTok{=} \DecValTok{1}

\NormalTok{        lon\_ticks }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ np.arange(np.ceil(bounds[}\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{]}\OperatorTok{/}\NormalTok{lon\_step)}\OperatorTok{*}\NormalTok{lon\_step, bounds[}\DecValTok{2}\NormalTok{], lon\_step)}
\NormalTok{        lat\_ticks }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ np.arange(np.ceil(bounds[}\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{]}\OperatorTok{/}\NormalTok{lat\_step)}\OperatorTok{*}\NormalTok{lat\_step, bounds[}\DecValTok{3}\NormalTok{], lat\_step)}

\NormalTok{        ax.set\_xticks(lon\_ticks)}
\NormalTok{        ax.set\_yticks(lat\_ticks)}
\NormalTok{        ax.set\_xticklabels([}\SpecialStringTok{f\textquotesingle{}}\SpecialCharTok{\{}\BuiltInTok{int}\NormalTok{(}\BuiltInTok{abs}\NormalTok{(x))}\SpecialCharTok{\}}\SpecialStringTok{°W\textquotesingle{}} \ControlFlowTok{for}\NormalTok{ x }\KeywordTok{in}\NormalTok{ lon\_ticks])}
\NormalTok{        ax.set\_yticklabels([}\SpecialStringTok{f\textquotesingle{}}\SpecialCharTok{\{}\BuiltInTok{int}\NormalTok{(y)}\SpecialCharTok{\}}\SpecialStringTok{°N\textquotesingle{}} \ControlFlowTok{for}\NormalTok{ y }\KeywordTok{in}\NormalTok{ lat\_ticks])}

\NormalTok{        ax.grid(}\VariableTok{True}\NormalTok{, alpha}\OperatorTok{=}\FloatTok{0.3}\NormalTok{, linewidth}\OperatorTok{=}\FloatTok{0.5}\NormalTok{, color}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}gray\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{        ax.set\_xlabel(}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}Longitude\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{        ax.set\_ylabel(}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}Latitude\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{)}
    \ControlFlowTok{else}\NormalTok{:}
\NormalTok{        ax.axis(}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}off\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{)}

    \CommentTok{\# Add colorbar}
\NormalTok{    sm }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ plt.cm.ScalarMappable(cmap}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{yield\_cmap,}
\NormalTok{                              norm}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{plt.Normalize(vmin}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{value\_ranges[}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}yield\_min\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{],}
\NormalTok{                                               vmax}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{value\_ranges[}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}yield\_max\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{]))}
\NormalTok{    cbar }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ plt.colorbar(sm, ax}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ax, shrink}\OperatorTok{=}\FloatTok{0.8}\NormalTok{, aspect}\OperatorTok{=}\DecValTok{30}\NormalTok{, pad}\OperatorTok{=}\FloatTok{0.02}\NormalTok{)}
    \CommentTok{\# cbar.set\_label(\textquotesingle{}Predicted Yield (bu/acre)\textquotesingle{}, rotation=90, labelpad=15)}

\NormalTok{    ax.set\_title(}\SpecialStringTok{f\textquotesingle{}Predicted Crop Yield (bu/ac) {-} }\SpecialCharTok{\{}\NormalTok{year}\SpecialCharTok{\}}\SpecialStringTok{\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, fontweight}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}bold\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, pad}\OperatorTok{=}\DecValTok{15}\NormalTok{)}

    \ControlFlowTok{return}\NormalTok{ fig, ax}

\KeywordTok{def}\NormalTok{ plot\_uncertainty(counties, year, value\_ranges, figsize}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{(}\DecValTok{8}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{6}\NormalTok{), show\_coords}\OperatorTok{=}\VariableTok{True}\NormalTok{):}
    \CommentTok{"""}
\CommentTok{    Create uncertainty map with less dense ticks}
\CommentTok{    """}
\NormalTok{    uncertainty\_colors }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ [}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}\#053061\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}\#2166ac\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}\#4393c3\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}\#92c5de\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}\#d1e5f0\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{,}
                         \StringTok{\textquotesingle{}\#f7f7f7\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}\#fddbc7\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}\#f4a582\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}\#d6604d\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}\#b2182b\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}\#67001f\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{]}
\NormalTok{    uncertainty\_cmap }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ LinearSegmentedColormap.from\_list(}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}uncertainty\_professional\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, uncertainty\_colors)}

\NormalTok{    fig, ax }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ plt.subplots(}\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{, figsize}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{figsize)}

    \CommentTok{\# Plot counties}
\NormalTok{    counties.boundary.plot(ax}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ax, linewidth}\OperatorTok{=}\FloatTok{0.2}\NormalTok{, color}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}\#666666\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, alpha}\OperatorTok{=}\FloatTok{0.8}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{    counties.plot(column}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}Uncertainty\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{,}
\NormalTok{                 ax}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ax,}
\NormalTok{                 cmap}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{uncertainty\_cmap,}
\NormalTok{                 vmin}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{value\_ranges[}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}uncertainty\_min\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{],}
\NormalTok{                 vmax}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{value\_ranges[}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}uncertainty\_max\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{],}
\NormalTok{                 edgecolor}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}none\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{)}

    \ControlFlowTok{if}\NormalTok{ show\_coords:}
\NormalTok{        bounds }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ counties.total\_bounds}
\NormalTok{        ax.set\_xlim(bounds[}\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{], bounds[}\DecValTok{2}\NormalTok{])}
\NormalTok{        ax.set\_ylim(bounds[}\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{], bounds[}\DecValTok{3}\NormalTok{])}

        \CommentTok{\# Create less dense coordinate ticks (same logic as yield plot)}
\NormalTok{        lon\_range }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ bounds[}\DecValTok{2}\NormalTok{] }\OperatorTok{{-}}\NormalTok{ bounds[}\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{]}
\NormalTok{        lat\_range }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ bounds[}\DecValTok{3}\NormalTok{] }\OperatorTok{{-}}\NormalTok{ bounds[}\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{]}

        \ControlFlowTok{if}\NormalTok{ lon\_range }\OperatorTok{\textgreater{}} \DecValTok{20}\NormalTok{:}
\NormalTok{            lon\_step }\OperatorTok{=} \DecValTok{5}    \CommentTok{\# Less dense}
\NormalTok{            lat\_step }\OperatorTok{=} \DecValTok{2}
        \ControlFlowTok{elif}\NormalTok{ lon\_range }\OperatorTok{\textgreater{}} \DecValTok{10}\NormalTok{:}
\NormalTok{            lon\_step }\OperatorTok{=} \DecValTok{3}
\NormalTok{            lat\_step }\OperatorTok{=} \DecValTok{2}
        \ControlFlowTok{else}\NormalTok{:}
\NormalTok{            lon\_step }\OperatorTok{=} \DecValTok{2}
\NormalTok{            lat\_step }\OperatorTok{=} \DecValTok{1}

\NormalTok{        lon\_ticks }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ np.arange(np.ceil(bounds[}\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{]}\OperatorTok{/}\NormalTok{lon\_step)}\OperatorTok{*}\NormalTok{lon\_step, bounds[}\DecValTok{2}\NormalTok{], lon\_step)}
\NormalTok{        lat\_ticks }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ np.arange(np.ceil(bounds[}\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{]}\OperatorTok{/}\NormalTok{lat\_step)}\OperatorTok{*}\NormalTok{lat\_step, bounds[}\DecValTok{3}\NormalTok{], lat\_step)}

\NormalTok{        ax.set\_xticks(lon\_ticks)}
\NormalTok{        ax.set\_yticks(lat\_ticks)}
\NormalTok{        ax.set\_xticklabels([}\SpecialStringTok{f\textquotesingle{}}\SpecialCharTok{\{}\BuiltInTok{int}\NormalTok{(}\BuiltInTok{abs}\NormalTok{(x))}\SpecialCharTok{\}}\SpecialStringTok{°W\textquotesingle{}} \ControlFlowTok{for}\NormalTok{ x }\KeywordTok{in}\NormalTok{ lon\_ticks])}
\NormalTok{        ax.set\_yticklabels([}\SpecialStringTok{f\textquotesingle{}}\SpecialCharTok{\{}\BuiltInTok{int}\NormalTok{(y)}\SpecialCharTok{\}}\SpecialStringTok{°N\textquotesingle{}} \ControlFlowTok{for}\NormalTok{ y }\KeywordTok{in}\NormalTok{ lat\_ticks])}

\NormalTok{        ax.grid(}\VariableTok{True}\NormalTok{, alpha}\OperatorTok{=}\FloatTok{0.3}\NormalTok{, linewidth}\OperatorTok{=}\FloatTok{0.5}\NormalTok{, color}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}gray\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{        ax.set\_xlabel(}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}Longitude\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{        ax.set\_ylabel(}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}Latitude\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{)}
    \ControlFlowTok{else}\NormalTok{:}
\NormalTok{        ax.axis(}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}off\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{)}

    \CommentTok{\# Add colorbar}
\NormalTok{    sm }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ plt.cm.ScalarMappable(cmap}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{uncertainty\_cmap,}
\NormalTok{                              norm}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{plt.Normalize(vmin}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{value\_ranges[}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}uncertainty\_min\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{],}
\NormalTok{                                               vmax}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{value\_ranges[}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}uncertainty\_max\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{]))}
\NormalTok{    cbar }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ plt.colorbar(sm, ax}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ax, shrink}\OperatorTok{=}\FloatTok{0.8}\NormalTok{, aspect}\OperatorTok{=}\DecValTok{30}\NormalTok{, pad}\OperatorTok{=}\FloatTok{0.02}\NormalTok{)}
    \CommentTok{\# cbar.set\_label(\textquotesingle{}Prediction Uncertainty (bu/acre)\textquotesingle{}, rotation=90, labelpad=15)}

\NormalTok{    ax.set\_title(}\SpecialStringTok{f\textquotesingle{}Prediction Uncertainty (bu/ac) {-} }\SpecialCharTok{\{}\NormalTok{year}\SpecialCharTok{\}}\SpecialStringTok{\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, fontweight}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}bold\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, pad}\OperatorTok{=}\DecValTok{15}\NormalTok{)}

    \ControlFlowTok{return}\NormalTok{ fig, ax}


\CommentTok{\# Calculate overall ranges once}
\NormalTok{value\_ranges }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ get\_overall\_ranges(df\_all, }\BuiltInTok{range}\NormalTok{(}\DecValTok{2018}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{2022}\NormalTok{))}

\CommentTok{\# Create separate plots for each year}
\ControlFlowTok{for}\NormalTok{ year }\KeywordTok{in} \BuiltInTok{range}\NormalTok{(}\DecValTok{2018}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{2022}\NormalTok{):}
    \CommentTok{\# Load the shapefile}
\NormalTok{    counties }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ gpd.read\_file(save\_path}\OperatorTok{+}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}cb\_2019\_us\_county\_20m.shp\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{)}

    \CommentTok{\# Get data for current year}
\NormalTok{    df\_year }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ df\_all[df\_all[}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}Year\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{] }\OperatorTok{==}\NormalTok{ year]}

    \CommentTok{\# Load and process X data}
\NormalTok{    X\_test }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ np.load(save\_path }\OperatorTok{+} \BuiltInTok{str}\NormalTok{(year) }\OperatorTok{+} \StringTok{\textquotesingle{}\_X.npy\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{    X\_test }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ normalize\_data(X\_test, X\_mean, X\_std)}

    \CommentTok{\# Get predictions}
\NormalTok{    predictions }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ model.predict(X\_test)}
\NormalTok{    mean\_pred }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ predictions[:, }\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{]}
\NormalTok{    std\_pred }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ predictions[:, }\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{]}

    \CommentTok{\# Add predictions to dataframe}
\NormalTok{    df\_year[}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}Predicted\_Yield\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{] }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ mean\_pred}
\NormalTok{    df\_year[}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}Uncertainty\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{] }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ std\_pred}

    \CommentTok{\# Merge data}
\NormalTok{    df\_year[}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}GEOID\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{] }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ df\_year[}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}GEOID\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{].astype(}\BuiltInTok{str}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{    counties[}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}GEOID\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{] }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ counties[}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}GEOID\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{].astype(}\BuiltInTok{str}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{    counties }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ counties.merge(df\_year, left\_on}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}GEOID\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, right\_on}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}GEOID\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{)}

    \CommentTok{\# Create and save yield prediction plot}
\NormalTok{    fig\_yield, ax\_yield }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ plot\_yield\_prediction(counties, year, value\_ranges)}
    \CommentTok{\# save\_individual\_figures(fig\_yield, year, \textquotesingle{}yield\_prediction\textquotesingle{}, save\_path)}
\NormalTok{    plt.show()}

    \CommentTok{\# Create and save uncertainty plot}

\NormalTok{    plt.show()}

\KeywordTok{def}\NormalTok{ plot\_annual\_average\_yields\_with\_std():}
    \CommentTok{"""}
\CommentTok{    Plot average actual vs predicted yields for each test year with standard deviation error bars}
\CommentTok{    """}
\NormalTok{    years }\OperatorTok{=} \BuiltInTok{list}\NormalTok{(}\BuiltInTok{range}\NormalTok{(}\DecValTok{2018}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{2022}\NormalTok{))}
\NormalTok{    avg\_actual\_yields }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ []}
\NormalTok{    avg\_predicted\_yields }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ []}
\NormalTok{    std\_actual\_yields }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ []}
\NormalTok{    std\_predicted\_yields }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ []}

    \CommentTok{\# Calculate average and std yields for each year}
    \ControlFlowTok{for}\NormalTok{ year }\KeywordTok{in}\NormalTok{ years:}
        \CommentTok{\# Load and process X data for the year}
\NormalTok{        X\_test\_year }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ np.load(save\_path }\OperatorTok{+} \BuiltInTok{str}\NormalTok{(year) }\OperatorTok{+} \StringTok{\textquotesingle{}\_X.npy\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{        X\_test\_year }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ normalize\_data(X\_test\_year, X\_mean, X\_std)}

        \CommentTok{\# Get predictions}
\NormalTok{        predictions }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ model.predict(X\_test\_year)}
\NormalTok{        pred\_yield }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ predictions[:, }\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{]}

        \CommentTok{\# Get actual yields for the year}
\NormalTok{        df\_year }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ df\_all[df\_all[}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}Year\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{] }\OperatorTok{==}\NormalTok{ year]}
\NormalTok{        true\_yield }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ df\_year[}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}Yield\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{].values}

        \CommentTok{\# Calculate averages and standard deviations}
\NormalTok{        avg\_actual }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ np.mean(true\_yield)}
\NormalTok{        avg\_predicted }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ np.mean(pred\_yield)}
\NormalTok{        std\_actual }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ np.std(true\_yield)}
\NormalTok{        std\_predicted }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ np.std(pred\_yield)}

\NormalTok{        avg\_actual\_yields.append(avg\_actual)}
\NormalTok{        avg\_predicted\_yields.append(avg\_predicted)}
\NormalTok{        std\_actual\_yields.append(std\_actual)}
\NormalTok{        std\_predicted\_yields.append(std\_predicted)}

        \BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(}\SpecialStringTok{f"Year }\SpecialCharTok{\{}\NormalTok{year}\SpecialCharTok{\}}\SpecialStringTok{:"}\NormalTok{)}
        \BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(}\SpecialStringTok{f"  Actual: }\SpecialCharTok{\{}\NormalTok{avg\_actual}\SpecialCharTok{:.2f\}}\SpecialStringTok{ ± }\SpecialCharTok{\{}\NormalTok{std\_actual}\SpecialCharTok{:.2f\}}\SpecialStringTok{ bu/ac"}\NormalTok{)}
        \BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(}\SpecialStringTok{f"  Predicted: }\SpecialCharTok{\{}\NormalTok{avg\_predicted}\SpecialCharTok{:.2f\}}\SpecialStringTok{ ± }\SpecialCharTok{\{}\NormalTok{std\_predicted}\SpecialCharTok{:.2f\}}\SpecialStringTok{ bu/ac"}\NormalTok{)}

    \CommentTok{\# Create the plot}
\NormalTok{    plt.figure(figsize}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{(}\DecValTok{10}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{6}\NormalTok{))}

    \CommentTok{\# Plot both lines with error bars}

    \CommentTok{\# Add legend}

    \CommentTok{\# Set y{-}axis to start from a reasonable minimum}

\NormalTok{    all\_stds }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ std\_actual\_yields }\OperatorTok{+}\NormalTok{ std\_predicted\_yields}
\NormalTok{    y\_min }\OperatorTok{=} \BuiltInTok{min}\NormalTok{(all\_values) }\OperatorTok{{-}} \BuiltInTok{max}\NormalTok{(all\_stds) }\OperatorTok{{-}} \DecValTok{2}
\NormalTok{    y\_max }\OperatorTok{=} \BuiltInTok{max}\NormalTok{(all\_values) }\OperatorTok{+} \BuiltInTok{max}\NormalTok{(all\_stds) }\OperatorTok{+} \DecValTok{2}
\NormalTok{    plt.ylim(y\_min, y\_max)}

    \CommentTok{\# Tight layout to prevent label cutoff}
\NormalTok{    plt.tight\_layout()}

    \CommentTok{\# Show the plot}
\NormalTok{    plt.show()}

    \CommentTok{\# Calculate and print overall statistics}
\NormalTok{    mae }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ np.mean(np.}\BuiltInTok{abs}\NormalTok{(np.array(avg\_actual\_yields) }\OperatorTok{{-}}\NormalTok{ np.array(avg\_predicted\_yields)))}

    \BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(}\SpecialStringTok{f"}\CharTok{\textbackslash{}n}\SpecialStringTok{Overall Performance Metrics (Annual Averages):"}\NormalTok{)}

    \CommentTok{\# Print standard deviation comparison}

  Actual: 51.28 ± 4.47 bu/ac
  Predicted: 50.54 ± 3.50 bu/ac
9/9 ━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━ 0s 5ms/step 
Year 2020:
  Actual: 52.36 ± 4.95 bu/ac
  Predicted: 50.73 ± 3.93 bu/ac
7/7 ━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━ 0s 6ms/step 
Year 2021:
  Actual: 52.65 ± 5.02 bu/ac
  Predicted: 53.26 ± 4.64 bu/ac
\end{verbatim}

\includegraphics{05_Time_Series/01__Soybean_Yield_Prediction/notebooks/Crop_yield_estimationR4_files/figure-pdf/cell-25-output-3.png}

\begin{verbatim}

Overall Performance Metrics (Annual Averages):
MAE: 0.96 bu/ac
RMSE: 1.04 bu/ac
R²: 0.398

Standard Deviation Comparison:
Average Std of Actual Yields: 4.81 bu/ac
Average Std of Predicted Yields: 4.18 bu/ac
\end{verbatim}

\section{Conclusions and Limitations}\label{conclusions-and-limitations}

This notebook demonstrated a phenology-guided, probabilistic deep
learning workflow for soybean yield prediction using satellite-derived
time series and environmental data. By aligning predictors with crop
developmental stages and employing probabilistic LSTM, 1D-CNN, and
Gaussian Process models, the approach captures both temporal yield
dynamics and associated prediction uncertainty. Results highlight the
dominant role of vegetation condition (EVI2), shallow soil moisture, and
late-season phenological stages in determining final yield.

Several limitations should be noted. The analysis is conducted at the
county level and does not explicitly incorporate detailed management
information such as planting dates, cultivar maturity groups, or
within-county variability. Additionally, while probabilistic outputs are
provided, uncertainty estimates are primarily aleatoric and would
benefit from further calibration and the explicit inclusion of epistemic
uncertainty. Readers are referred to the accompanying book chapter for a
more detailed discussion of results, interpretation, and methodological
considerations.

\section{Looking Forward: Future Directions and Next
Steps}\label{looking-forward-future-directions-and-next-steps}

This notebook has walked through a phenology‑guided deep learning
pipeline for soybean yield prediction, including probabilistic modeling
of prediction uncertainty. If you are relatively new to deep learning in
agriculture, being able to train a time‑series model that predicts yield
and provides its own uncertainty estimates is already a significant
step.

As discussed in the limitations and future directions of the chapter,
this framework is meant to serve as a foundation rather than an
endpoint. When adapting these ideas to your own work, you might consider
the following directions:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \textbf{Exploring Advanced Time Series Architectures:} You can
  experiment by varying the current LSTM architecture (e.g., adjusting
  the number of layers, units, or regularization) to observe how model
  complexity impacts performance and overfitting. Additionally, consider
  comparing the LSTM against other sequence models like Gated Recurrent
  Units (GRUs) or modern Attention/Transformer-based architectures. As
  an analytical exercise, you might also investigate early-season
  forecasting by training models using only the first few phenological
  stages to determine exactly when reliable yield signals first emerge.
\item
  \textbf{Expanding Phenology Applications:} The satellite-derived
  phenology approach used here is highly adaptable. You can modify this
  pipeline to study different crops (such as corn or wheat) by swapping
  the MODIS transition dates, or test the integration of specific
  extreme weather proxies, like heat-stress degree days or drought
  indices.
\item
  \textbf{Deepening Uncertainty Quantification (UQ):} The probabilistic
  models in this notebook output a mean and variance to capture
  \emph{aleatoric} uncertainty (inherent noise in the data). A powerful
  next step is to explore \emph{epistemic} uncertainty (uncertainty in
  the model's own knowledge). You could try implementing \textbf{Deep
  Ensembles} (training multiple models and combining their predictive
  distributions) or exploring \textbf{Bayesian Neural Networks} (e.g.,
  using TensorFlow Probability) to generate even more robust,
  trustworthy confidence intervals for decision-makers.
\end{itemize}

Ultimately, the goal of these tools is to align with initiatives like
the NASA Earth Science to Action framework---translating raw satellite
data into actionable agricultural insights. We encourage you to adapt
this code to your own local regions and continue exploring how machine
learning can support sustainable, climate-resilient agriculture!

\part{Future of Deep Learning and Foundational Models}

\chapter{Evaluating Foundation Models Trained with Earth Observation
Data}\label{evaluating-foundation-models-trained-with-earth-observation-data}

\href{https://colab.research.google.com/github/NASA-EarthRISE/EarthRISE-Applied-Artificial-Intelligence-and-Deep-Learning-Book/blob/main/10_Future/01_Evaluating_Foundation_Models_Trained_with_Earth_Observation_Data/Chapter_Practical_EOFM_Evaluation.ipynb}{\includegraphics{index_files/mediabag/colab_logo_32px.png}
Run in Colab}

Foundation models for Earth observation (EO) have the capacity to
transform how critical real-world challenges are approached---from rapid
flood mapping during disasters to monitoring deforestation and assessing
agricultural health. By learning general-purpose representations from
vast amounts of satellite imagery, these models can be quickly adapted
to new tasks with minimal labeled data, dramatically reducing the time
and cost of developing specialized applications. This chapter explores
how to apply and evaluate these powerful tools for practical Earth
science applications.

\begin{center}\rule{0.5\linewidth}{0.5pt}\end{center}

\section{Introduction}\label{introduction-1}

A foundation model for Earth observation (EO) data is a large-scale
machine learning model pre-trained on vast amounts of remote sensing
data from various satellites and sensors, designed to learn
general-purpose representations of the Earth's surface and atmospheric
phenomena. These models can capture diverse features from a variety of
EO datasets, such as optical, radar, thermal, and multispectral sensor
imagery, and can be fine-tuned for specific tasks like environmental
monitoring, land use classification, climate analysis, and disaster
response.

Most current foundation models are developed using a technique called
\emph{self-supervised learning}, in which models learn useful
representations by solving tasks that are derived from the structure of
the input data itself, without relying on human-annotated labels. These
tasks, known as \emph{pretext tasks}, are designed to uncover internal
patterns or latent features within the data distribution by creating a
supervisory signal from the data alone. A common example is masked
reconstruction, where parts of the input are intentionally hidden or
removed, and the model is trained to predict or reconstruct the missing
content based on the visible portions. This encourages the model to
capture the underlying structure and context of the data. Other
approaches to self-supervision include contrastive learning, where the
model learns to distinguish between similar and dissimilar pairs of
data, and generative methods, which learn to synthesize or model entire
input distributions. Unlike traditional supervised learning, which
relies on labeled datasets, self-supervised learning does not require
labeled examples (\href{https://doi.org/10.48550/arXiv.2002.05709}{Chen
et al., 2020}). Self-supervised learning can be applied in many types of
modeling tasks, including computer vision, and is gaining traction
because it allows models to leverage vast amounts of unlabeled data
whilst showing to be effective in improving performance across various
domains.

\textbf{Figure 1.} A general self-supervised framework for remote
sensing image classification, illustrating how models learn
representations from unlabeled data through pretext tasks.

\emph{Source: \href{https://doi.org/10.3390/rs14194824}{``A General
Self-Supervised Framework for Remote Sensing Image Classification'' by
Gao et al., 2022.}}

Vision Transformers (ViTs) are a class of deep learning architectures
that apply the transformer framework---originally developed for natural
language processing---to visual data. By dividing images into fixed-size
patches and treating them as a sequence of tokens, ViTs model patterns
and relationships in the data using self-attention mechanisms. While
ViTs are commonly used as the architectural backbone in many vision
foundation models, they are not foundation models by themselves. Rather,
ViTs serve as one of the key building blocks in constructing such
models. ViTs require large datasets because transformers do not have the
built-in inductive biases (like translation invariance) that
convolutional neural networks (CNNs) have
(\href{https://doi.org/10.48550/arXiv.2010.11929}{Dosovitskiy et al.,
2021}). The data provided to train these models must be crafted with
thoughtful sampling and stratification to ensure the correct
representations are learned, and biases or artifacts reduced. Once that
is ensured, it is worth considering that the effectiveness of these
models greatly improves with larger datasets by provisioning more
examples and greater diversity, thereby allowing the model to learn
robust representations that generalize well across different tasks. More
data improves the learning signal and helps the model differentiate
between subtle visual cues
(\href{https://doi.org/10.48550/arXiv.2002.05709}{Chen et al., 2020}).

\textbf{Figure 2.} Transformer-based architecture for land use
classification. \emph{Source:
\href{https://doi.org/10.1038/s41598-024-67186-4}{``Transformer-based
land use and land cover classification with explainability using
satellite imagery'' by Khan et al., 2024}}

A foundation model's extensive training regime enables its applicability
across various downstream tasks. To achieve a properly trained FM,
exposure to diverse and extensive data is needed in order to capture the
variability and complexity inherent in real-world settings. For
vision-specific foundation models, this means learning representations
that can differentiate between millions of different phenomena, scenes,
textures, lighting conditions, and other visual phenomena
(\href{https://doi.org/10.48550/arXiv.2010.11929}{Dosovitskiy et al.,
2021}). Large datasets help foundation models handle data bias and
distribution shifts. For remote sensing vision tasks, biases can come
from the sensor type, camera angle, geographic location, weather and
lighting conditions under which images were captured
(\href{https://doi.org/10.48550/arXiv.2103.00020}{Radford et al.,
2021}). Training on diverse datasets that encompass multiple contexts
and environments helps the model learn robust representations that
generalize well across different conditions. Furthermore, training large
models with millions (or billions) of parameters requires substantial
data to avoid overfitting. When training vision-specific models, such as
those using ViTs as the underlying architecture or applying a
self-supervised learning paradigm, having large datasets ensures that
the model does not merely memorize the training data but learns
generalized features that are useful across tasks
(\href{https://doi.org/10.48550/arXiv.2010.11929}{Dosovitskiy et al.,
2021}). Yet, while large datasets can support the learning of
generalized features rather than memorization, increasing dataset size
alone does not guarantee generalization---especially as models grow in
parameter count. Since larger models also have a higher capacity to
memorize training data, which can lead to overfitting, the task of
properly sampling and splitting the data is crucial. For practitioners,
this means that a biased or overfitted model could produce unreliable
predictions---for example, a flood detection model trained primarily on
temperate regions might fail catastrophically when applied to
monsoon-affected areas in South Asia. Ensuring meaningful generalization
requires careful dataset sampling and experimental design. Researchers
must ensure that evaluation data (e.g., test sets) contain spatial
and/or temporal slices not seen during training to mitigate data leakage
and provide more robust evidence that performance stems from
generalization, not memorization.

As stated, many vision foundation models rely on self-supervised
learning, where they generate their own supervision signals from the
data itself by solving pretext tasks---such as predicting masked patches
in an image or distinguishing between augmented views. While large
datasets offer the diversity needed to expose models to a broad range of
visual patterns and contexts, dataset size alone is not sufficient. The
quality, representativeness, and sampling strategy of the data are
equally critical. Poorly sampled or imbalanced data can bias the model
or lead to overfitting, especially in self-supervised setups where the
supervision signal comes entirely from the data distribution. Careful
attention must be paid to spatial and temporal coverage, stratification
across relevant factors (e.g., class distributions, lighting conditions,
sensor types), and avoidance of data leakage. High-quality, well-curated
datasets not only enhance learning but also ensure that the
representations learned are robust, transferable, and capable of
capturing fine-grained details like textures, colors, and shapes across
diverse conditions.

\begin{center}\rule{0.5\linewidth}{0.5pt}\end{center}

\subsection{How foundation models improve efficiency in machine learning
workflows}\label{how-foundation-models-improve-efficiency-in-machine-learning-workflows}

Foundation models improve efficiency in machine learning workflows by
enabling tasks such as zero-shot inference, transfer learning, and
general-purpose feature extraction with minimal task-specific data or
training. These theoretical benefits have been demonstrated in practical
applications across domains. For example,
\href{https://huggingface.co/collections/ibm-nasa-geospatial/prithvi-for-earth-observation-6740a7a81883466bf41d93d6}{Prithvi
(Prithvi-EO-1.0 and 2.0)} has shown state-of-the-art performance in
flood detection and above ground biomass estimation with limited labeled
data (\href{https://doi.org/10.48550/arXiv.2310.18660}{Jakubik et al.,
2023} and \href{https://doi.org/10.48550/arXiv.2412.02732}{Szwarcman et
al., 2025}).
\href{https://clay-foundation.github.io/model/index.html}{CLAY} has
demonstrated robust zero-shot generalization across different
geographies and resolutions. For example, it enabled zero shot detection
of deforestation events in
\href{https://aws.amazon.com/blogs/machine-learning/revolutionizing-earth-observation-with-geospatial-foundation-models-on-aws/}{Schroer
et al., 2025}. These efficiencies translate to significant reductions in
labeling costs and compute resources, especially in data-scarce or
resource-constrained settings.

\subsubsection{\texorpdfstring{\textbf{1. Reduced training
costs}}{1. Reduced training costs}}\label{reduced-training-costs}

Traditional models often require training from scratch for every
application, demanding significant computational resources and labeled
datasets, which is especially challenging in earth observation contexts.
Foundation models are pre-trained on massive datasets and can be
fine-tuned for specific tasks using smaller labeled datasets,
drastically reducing the costs of data annotation and training. Since
these models undergo extensive pre-training on diverse datasets, they
allow for the advantage of transfer learning to enable high performance
with limited fine-tuning using smaller, domain-specific datasets.
Suffice to say, foundation models reduce barriers to entry for users
with limited resources.

\textbf{Example:} Instead of creating a wholly new model for
deforestation detection, a pre-trained foundation model can be adapted
in a fraction of the time and cost.

\subsubsection{\texorpdfstring{\textbf{2. Faster
development}}{2. Faster development}}\label{faster-development}

Foundation models accelerate the development cycle by providing
ready-to-use features or embeddings. This eliminates the need for
extensive preprocessing and training, allowing users to focus on
fine-tuning or directly deploying the model.

\textbf{Example:} A user working with PCA analysis on hurricane impacts
can use pre-trained embeddings from a foundation model, reducing the
need to engineer complex features.

\subsubsection{\texorpdfstring{\textbf{3. Improved accuracy with limited
data}}{3. Improved accuracy with limited data}}\label{improved-accuracy-with-limited-data}

Foundation models benefit from their extensive pre-training, making them
robust and effective even when fine-tuned with limited domain-specific
data. This reduces the need for expensive and time-consuming field
campaigns or data collection.

\textbf{Example:} Foundation models trained with weather data may have
learned to account for confounding weather variation that would
otherwise have to be learned from the extensive collection and labeling
of target features under different weather conditions.

\subsubsection{\texorpdfstring{\textbf{4. Scalability across
applications}}{4. Scalability across applications}}\label{scalability-across-applications}

A single foundation model can be repurposed for various tasks, avoiding
the cost of building separate models for each application.

\textbf{Example:} A foundation model trained on global land cover data
can be applied to soil health assessment, mangrove monitoring, or coral
reef health analysis.

\subsubsection{\texorpdfstring{\textbf{5. Democratizing
access}}{5. Democratizing access}}\label{democratizing-access}

With pre-trained foundation models available as open-source or through
APIs, smaller organizations and researchers gain access to high-quality
tools without investing in expensive infrastructure.

\subsubsection{\texorpdfstring{\textbf{6. Good backbones (better than
ImageNet or RGB-trained
models)}}{6. Good backbones (better than ImageNet or RGB-trained models)}}\label{good-backbones-better-than-imagenet-or-rgb-trained-models}

Foundation models for EO are trained on specialized geospatial datasets
(e.g.~multi-spectral or radar satellite imagery), making their feature
representations more aligned with EO tasks compared to models
pre-trained on ImageNet or similar RGB datasets. EO data often includes
multi-spectral bands (e.g., near-infrared) or radar data, which contain
richer and more complex information than standard RGB images. -
\textbf{Example:} A foundation model trained on multi-spectral imagery
will perform better for tasks like vegetation health assessment than an
RGB-trained ImageNet model.

\subsubsection{\texorpdfstring{\textbf{7. Not as good as bespoke models,
but get 90\% there with 10\% of the
work}}{7. Not as good as bespoke models, but get 90\% there with 10\% of the work}}\label{not-as-good-as-bespoke-models-but-get-90-there-with-10-of-the-work}

While foundation models may not achieve the same level of accuracy as
task-specific bespoke models, they deliver competitive results with a
fraction of the effort. Bespoke models demand extensive tuning, custom
architectures, and domain expertise, which are costly and
time-intensive. Foundation models offer a practical compromise. -
\textbf{Example:} For deforestation detection, a bespoke model might
achieve 95\% accuracy, while a foundation model could achieve 90\%
accuracy with significantly less effort.

\begin{center}\rule{0.5\linewidth}{0.5pt}\end{center}

\subsection{Limitations}\label{limitations}

All this said, foundation models are still a relatively new development
in EO and have yet to see widespread validation across diverse
real-world applications. Thus, while promising, their adoption is still
in its early stages. Challenges like model bias, scalability, and
performance in extreme conditions require further study. For instance,
in precision agriculture, models may struggle with crop varieties not
well-represented in training data; in urban planning, rapidly changing
cityscapes may not be captured; and in polar research, the unique
spectral signatures of ice and snow can confound models trained
primarily on temperate regions. - \textbf{Example:} A foundation model
trained on global land cover data might need more validation to ensure
reliability in highly localized or unique environments.

\begin{center}\rule{0.5\linewidth}{0.5pt}\end{center}

\subsection{Challenges}\label{challenges}

There are multiple aspects of Earth Observation Foundation Models (EOFM)
that are unique to the broader scope of foundation models research.
Earth observation imagery is very diverse in how it is captured. The
instruments capture various frequencies at different wavelength and band
widths, the ground sampling distance of the captured imagery ranges from
a few centimeters to hundreds of meters, and the same location is often
captured at multiple points in time. These substantial differences in
the spectral, spatial, and temporal resolution make it harder for models
to learn generalized patterns. In addition, earth observation data also
has unique metadata characteristics that can be deterministic for the
quality and the potential content of a dataset. Examples are the
coordinate reference system used, the latitude and longitude location of
the captured image, view angles that may be different from image to
image, and data provenance artifacts such as how the data is stored and
served across various providers.

Foundation models in EO would ideally capture and learn from as many of
the key dimensions and metadata items as possible. An ideal model would
be trained on a wide range of variation in these parameters (spatial,
spectral, temporal resolutions, as well as geography, for example)
during the learning process, via methods like global sampling over
multiple years and seasons, as well as for a wide range of different
sensors at different resolutions. Similarly, models would ideally accept
a wide range of formats and image sizes during inference.

While no single model to date fully integrates all relevant dimensions
of remote sensing complexity---such as temporal dynamics, multi-sensor
fusion, and variable spatial and spectral resolutions---there are
promising efforts in each area: for instance, Prithvi and
\href{https://doi.org/10.48550/arXiv.2304.14065}{Presto} explore
temporal modeling,
\href{https://doi.org/10.48550/arXiv.2403.15356}{DOFA} addresses
multi-sensor fusion, and Clay supports variable image resolutions and
types. However, the goal need not be a single, monolithic model that
captures all aspects simultaneously. In practice, combining multiple
specialized models in modular or hierarchical architectures can offer a
more scalable and adaptable approach. These modular systems can
collaboratively represent the rich diversity of the remote sensing
domain, allowing for flexible subsetting, expansion, and fine-tuning
across tasks. It's important to think creatively about architecture
design, embracing hybrid strategies that align with the diverse and
evolving nature of geospatial data.

\begin{center}\rule{0.5\linewidth}{0.5pt}\end{center}

\begin{quote}
\textbf{⚠️ GPU Required:} This notebook uses GPU acceleration for
running foundation models. Please ensure you are using a GPU runtime
(e.g., in Google Colab: Runtime → Change runtime type → GPU). If you are
running low on compute units, be aware that model loading and inference
steps will require GPU resources.
\end{quote}

\subsubsection{Using and evaluating
EOFM}\label{using-and-evaluating-eofm}

In the next portion, we will work through interactive demonstrations of
how to use and evaluate EOFM for real domain-specific downstream tasks.
Specifically, we will leverage two different EOFMs to generate
embeddings for imagery before and after a natural hazard, and determine
if these embeddings capture a descriptive pattern. We will also use
these EOFMs in a fine tuning capacity to detect the natural hazard.
After fine tuning, we'll calculate the performance scores. At the end,
you will have worked through two different methods of leveraging EOFM
using two different EOFM base models.

The two models we will work with include: - \textbf{Prithvi} ---
NASA/IBM's temporal Vision Transformer trained on Harmonized
Landsat-Sentinel-2 (HLS) data, optimized for spatiotemporal analysis of
Earth observation imagery. - \textbf{Clay} --- A flexible EOFM
supporting variable image resolutions and sensor types, designed for
broad applicability across diverse remote sensing tasks.

To begin evaluating the EOFMs, we will start with the Prithvi model.

The \href{https://github.com/NASA-IMPACT/Prithvi-EO-2.0}{Prithvi HLS
foundation model} is an EOFM developed collaboratively by IBM and NASA.
It leverages a \textbf{Vision Transformer (ViT)} architecture and is
pre-trained on the \href{https://hls.gsfc.nasa.gov/}{Harmonized
Landsat-Sentinel-2 (HLS)} dataset. The model incorporates a
\textbf{Masked Autoencoder (MAE)} self-supervised learning approach. Its
design is optimized for spatiotemporal analysis, using 3D patch
embeddings to encode spatial and temporal features from input data
formatted as a sequence of geospatial ``videos''
(\texttt{B,\ C,\ T,\ H,\ W} --- bands, channels, time, height, width).

The architecture leverages temporal embeddings to model time-series
dynamics and spatial embeddings to capture geospatial structure.
Spectral inputs to Prithvi include six key spectral bands---Blue, Green,
Red, Narrow NIR, SWIR1, and SWIR2---provided as geotiff files in
reflectance units.

\textbf{Figure 3.} Prithvi foundation model architecture showing the
masked autoencoder approach.

\emph{Source:
\href{https://huggingface.co/ibm-nasa-geospatial/Prithvi-EO-2.0-300M}{Hugging
Face - Prithvi-EO-2.0-300M}}

It has been fine-tuned for applications such as flood mapping, burn scar
detection, and land cover classification. The pre-trained weights and
fine-tuning workflows are openly available on platforms like Hugging
Face, promoting accessibility and further development by the research
community.

Prithvi 2.0 Citation:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\VariableTok{@article}\NormalTok{\{}\OtherTok{Prithvi}\NormalTok{{-}}\OtherTok{EO}\NormalTok{{-}}\OtherTok{V2}\NormalTok{{-}}\OtherTok{preprint}\NormalTok{,    }
    \DataTypeTok{author}\NormalTok{          = \{Szwarcman, Daniela and Roy, Sujit and Fraccaro, Paolo and Gíslason, Þorsteinn Elí and Blumenstiel, Benedikt and Ghosal, Rinki and de Oliveira, Pedro Henrique and de Sousa Almeida, João Lucas and Sedona, Rocco and Kang, Yanghui and Chakraborty, Srija and Wang, Sizhe and Kumar, Ankur and Truong, Myscon and Godwin, Denys and Lee, Hyunho and Hsu, Chia{-}Yu and Akbari Asanjan, Ata and Mujeci, Besart and Keenan, Trevor and Arévolo, Paulo and Li, Wenwen and Alemohammad, Hamed and Olofsson, Pontus and Hain, Christopher and Kennedy, Robert and Zadrozny, Bianca and Cavallaro, Gabriele and Watson, Campbell and Maskey, Manil and Ramachandran, Rahul and Bernabe Moreno, Juan\},}
    \DataTypeTok{title}\NormalTok{           = \{\{Prithvi{-}EO{-}2.0: A Versatile Multi{-}Temporal Foundation Model for Earth Observation Applications\}\},}
    \DataTypeTok{journal}\NormalTok{         = \{arXiv preprint arXiv:2412.02732\},}
    \DataTypeTok{year}\NormalTok{            = \{2024\}}
\NormalTok{\}}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\section{Run Prithvi}\label{run-prithvi}

This exercise involves carrying out a complete analysis from beginning
to end. The steps include: 1. Selecting a location and time range of
interest. 2. Downloading \href{https://hls.gsfc.nasa.gov/}{Harmonized
Landsat and Sentinel-2 (HLS)} imagery for the specified parameters (make
sure you have an \href{https://urs.earthdata.nasa.gov/}{Earth Data
login} and/or
\href{https://urs.earthdata.nasa.gov/documentation/for_users/data_access/create_net_rc_file}{.netrc
file} for this). 3. Loading the model checkpoint. 4. Formatting the data
to match the model's requirements. 5. Running the model on the prepared
imagery. 6. Performing PCA and TSNE to analyze the model's output
(embeddings). 7. Train a classifier on top of the model's embeddings.

do not need any external requirements file, just these two lines below.

\subsection{Define Location and Date of
Interest}\label{define-location-and-date-of-interest}

For this example, we analyze a region in Pakistan that experienced a
monsoon flood. We will generate embeddings for the area with the model
and analyze any relationships within those.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\CommentTok{\# Point over Padidan, Pakistan}
\NormalTok{lat, lon }\OperatorTok{=} \FloatTok{26.776567}\NormalTok{, }\FloatTok{68.287374}

\CommentTok{\# Dates surrounding a major monsoon flood (August 20, 2022)}
\NormalTok{start }\OperatorTok{=} \StringTok{"2022{-}06{-}01"}
\NormalTok{end }\OperatorTok{=} \StringTok{"2022{-}10{-}30"}

\subsection{Natural Disaster in Focus}\label{natural-disaster-in-focus}

The
\href{https://floodlist.com/asia/pakistan-monsoon-floods-august-2022}{2022
Pakistan floods} stand as one of the most catastrophic flood events in
recent history. Between
\href{https://agupubs.onlinelibrary.wiley.com/doi/full/10.1029/2022EF003230}{July
and August 2022}, unprecedented monsoon rainfall triggered massive
flooding across Pakistan, affecting
\href{https://www.worldbank.org/en/news/press-release/2022/10/28/pakistan-flood-damages-and-economic-losses-over-usd-30-billion-and-reconstruction-needs-over-usd-16-billion-new-assessme}{approximately
33 million people} and inundating much of the country. Vast agricultural
lands were submerged, millions were displaced, and critical
infrastructure was destroyed.

Organizations like \href{https://www.icimod.org/}{ICIMOD} played a vital
role in disaster response, providing satellite-based assessments of crop
losses and flood extent
(\href{https://lib.icimod.org/records/qv11m-0wm93}{ICIMOD Flood
Assessment}). By applying EOFMs to this use case, we demonstrate how
foundation models can rapidly support flood mapping and damage
assessment---capabilities that are essential for timely humanitarian
response and post-disaster recovery planning.

\subsubsection{Region of Interest: Padidan,
Pakistan}\label{region-of-interest-padidan-pakistan}

Our study area is centered on Padidan in Pakistan's Sindh Province,
\href{https://floodlist.com/asia/pakistan-monsoon-floods-august-2022}{one
of the regions most severely impacted by the floods}. During this
period, the Indus River and its tributaries overflowed, submerging the
surrounding agricultural lands and displacing local communities.

\textbf{Key References:} -
\href{https://floodlist.com/asia/pakistan-monsoon-floods-august-2022}{FloodList:
Pakistan Monsoon Floods August 2022} -
\href{https://agupubs.onlinelibrary.wiley.com/doi/full/10.1029/2022EF003230}{AGU:
Climate and Hydrological Analysis of the 2022 Pakistan Floods} -
\href{https://www.worldbank.org/en/news/press-release/2022/10/28/pakistan-flood-damages-and-economic-losses-over-usd-30-billion-and-reconstruction-needs-over-usd-16-billion-new-assessme}{World
Bank: Pakistan Flood Damages Assessment} -
\href{https://lib.icimod.org/records/qv11m-0wm93}{ICIMOD Library: Flood
Assessment}

This historical context underscores the importance of rapid, accurate
flood mapping---a task well-suited for Earth Observation Foundation
Models.

\subsection{Retrieve Data from the STAC
Catalog}\label{retrieve-data-from-the-stac-catalog}

Using the specified location and dates, let's obtain
\href{https://hls.gsfc.nasa.gov/}{Harmonized Landsat and Sentinel-2
(HLS)} imagery with \texttt{stackstac}. We'll parameterize our search
query so as to only retrieve the desired STAC items for analysis.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{STAC\_API }\OperatorTok{=} \StringTok{"https://cmr.earthdata.nasa.gov/stac/LPCLOUD"}
\NormalTok{COLLECTION }\OperatorTok{=} \StringTok{"HLSS30.v2.0"}

\CommentTok{\# Search the catalogue}
\NormalTok{catalog }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ pystac\_client.Client.}\BuiltInTok{open}\NormalTok{(STAC\_API)}

\NormalTok{search }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ catalog.search(}
\NormalTok{    collections}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{[COLLECTION],}
\NormalTok{    datetime}\OperatorTok{=}\SpecialStringTok{f"}\SpecialCharTok{\{}\NormalTok{start}\SpecialCharTok{\}}\SpecialStringTok{/}\SpecialCharTok{\{}\NormalTok{end}\SpecialCharTok{\}}\SpecialStringTok{"}\NormalTok{,}
\NormalTok{    intersects}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{mapping(Point(lon, lat)) ,}
\NormalTok{    max\_items}\OperatorTok{=}\DecValTok{100}\NormalTok{,}
\NormalTok{    query}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{\{}\StringTok{"eo:cloud\_cover"}\NormalTok{: \{}\StringTok{"lt"}\NormalTok{: }\DecValTok{50}\NormalTok{\}\},}
\NormalTok{)}

\NormalTok{all\_items }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{  search.item\_collection()}
\NormalTok{all\_items}
\NormalTok{item }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ all\_items[}\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{]}
\NormalTok{item}

\CommentTok{\# Use S3 links for downloading imagery}
\ControlFlowTok{for}\NormalTok{ item }\KeywordTok{in}\NormalTok{ all\_items:}
    \ControlFlowTok{for}\NormalTok{ key }\KeywordTok{in}\NormalTok{ item.assets.keys():}
        \ControlFlowTok{if} \StringTok{"alternate"} \KeywordTok{in}\NormalTok{ item.assets[key].extra\_fields:}
\NormalTok{            url }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ urllib.parse.urlparse(}
\NormalTok{                item.assets[key].extra\_fields[}\StringTok{"alternate"}\NormalTok{][}\StringTok{"s3"}\NormalTok{][}\StringTok{"href"}\NormalTok{]}
\NormalTok{            )}
\NormalTok{            item.assets[key].href }\OperatorTok{=} \SpecialStringTok{f"https://}\SpecialCharTok{\{}\NormalTok{url}\SpecialCharTok{.}\NormalTok{netloc}\SpecialCharTok{\}}\SpecialStringTok{.s3.amazonaws.com}\SpecialCharTok{\{}\NormalTok{url}\SpecialCharTok{.}\NormalTok{path}\SpecialCharTok{\}}\SpecialStringTok{"}
\NormalTok{            item.assets[key].href }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ item.assets[key].extra\_fields[}\StringTok{"alternate"}\NormalTok{][}\StringTok{"s3"}\NormalTok{][}
                \StringTok{"href"}
\NormalTok{            ]}

\NormalTok{items }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ []}
\NormalTok{dates }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ []}
\ControlFlowTok{for}\NormalTok{ item }\KeywordTok{in}\NormalTok{ all\_items:}
\NormalTok{    items.append(item)}
\NormalTok{    dates.append(item.datetime.date())}


\BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(}\SpecialStringTok{f"Found }\SpecialCharTok{\{}\BuiltInTok{len}\NormalTok{(items)}\SpecialCharTok{\}}\SpecialStringTok{ items"}\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

Found 22 items
\end{verbatim}

\subsection{Create a Bounding Box for the Area of
Interest}\label{create-a-bounding-box-for-the-area-of-interest}

We'll generate this bounding box in the projection of the dataset to
ensure appropriately sized image chips.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\CommentTok{\# Extract coordinate system}
\NormalTok{epsg }\OperatorTok{=} \DecValTok{32642} \CommentTok{\# For Padidan, Pakistan.}

\CommentTok{\# Convert point of interest into the image projection}
\CommentTok{\# (assumes all images are in the same projection)}
\NormalTok{poidf }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ gpd.GeoDataFrame(}
\NormalTok{    pd.DataFrame(),}
\NormalTok{    crs}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"EPSG:4326"}\NormalTok{,}
\NormalTok{    geometry}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{[Point(lon, lat)],}
\NormalTok{).to\_crs(epsg)}

\CommentTok{\# Get lat and lon in meters (UTM), where lat and lon are at the center of our intended image chip.}
\NormalTok{coords }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ poidf.iloc[}\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{].geometry.coords[}\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{]}

\CommentTok{\# Create bounds in projection}
\NormalTok{size }\OperatorTok{=} \DecValTok{256}
\NormalTok{gsd }\OperatorTok{=} \DecValTok{30}
\CommentTok{\# This creates a bounding box of 256 × 30m = 7680m × 7680m (7.68 km square), centered on the point.}
\NormalTok{bounds }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ (}
\NormalTok{    coords[}\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{] }\OperatorTok{{-}}\NormalTok{ (size }\OperatorTok{*}\NormalTok{ gsd) }\OperatorTok{//} \DecValTok{2}\NormalTok{,}
\NormalTok{    coords[}\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{] }\OperatorTok{{-}}\NormalTok{ (size }\OperatorTok{*}\NormalTok{ gsd) }\OperatorTok{//} \DecValTok{2}\NormalTok{,}
\NormalTok{    coords[}\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{] }\OperatorTok{+}\NormalTok{ (size }\OperatorTok{*}\NormalTok{ gsd) }\OperatorTok{//} \DecValTok{2}\NormalTok{,}
\NormalTok{    coords[}\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{] }\OperatorTok{+}\NormalTok{ (size }\OperatorTok{*}\NormalTok{ gsd) }\OperatorTok{//} \DecValTok{2}\NormalTok{,}
\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\subsection{Retrieve the imagery data}\label{retrieve-the-imagery-data}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\CommentTok{\# Retrieve the pixel values, for the bounding box in}
\CommentTok{\# the target projection. In this example we use the}
\CommentTok{\# RGB, NIR and SWIR bands.}
\CommentTok{\# Clip the imagery to the defined bounds.}
\CommentTok{\# Use nearest{-}neighbor resampling to avoid interpolation artifacts.}
\NormalTok{stack }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ stackstac.stack(}
\NormalTok{    items,}
\NormalTok{    bounds}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{bounds,}
\NormalTok{    snap\_bounds}\OperatorTok{=}\VariableTok{False}\NormalTok{,}
\NormalTok{    epsg}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{epsg,}
\NormalTok{    resolution}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{gsd,}
\NormalTok{    dtype}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"float64"}\NormalTok{,}
\NormalTok{    rescale}\OperatorTok{=}\VariableTok{False}\NormalTok{,}
\NormalTok{    fill\_value}\OperatorTok{=}\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{,}
\NormalTok{    assets}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{[}\StringTok{"B02"}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{"B03"}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{"B04"}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{"B05"}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{"B06"}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{"B07"}\NormalTok{],}
\NormalTok{    resampling}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{Resampling.nearest,}
\NormalTok{)}

\BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(}\SpecialStringTok{f"Working with stack of size }\SpecialCharTok{\{}\NormalTok{stack}\SpecialCharTok{.}\NormalTok{shape}\SpecialCharTok{\}}\SpecialStringTok{"}\NormalTok{)}

\NormalTok{stack }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ stack.compute()}

\NormalTok{stack}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\subsection{Review the Downloaded
Imagery}\label{review-the-downloaded-imagery}

The imagery dataset contains four pre-flood images two during-flood
images (one of which is cloudy) and 12 post-flood images.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{stack.sel(band}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{[}\StringTok{"B04"}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{"B03"}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{"B02"}\NormalTok{]).plot.imshow(}
\NormalTok{    row}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"time"}\NormalTok{, rgb}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"band"}\NormalTok{, vmin}\OperatorTok{=}\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{, vmax}\OperatorTok{=}\DecValTok{2000}\NormalTok{, col\_wrap}\OperatorTok{=}\DecValTok{6}
\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}
