\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{stack.shape}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\subsection{Load the Model}\label{load-the-model}

Now that we have the imagery prepared, the next step is to load the
model for analysis.

\textbf{Estimated Time:} Model loading typically takes 2-5 minutes
depending on your internet connection, as the model weights
(\textasciitilde1.3 GB) are downloaded from Hugging Face.

\textbf{Hugging Face Authentication (Optional but Recommended):} If you
encounter authentication errors or timeouts when loading the model, you
can authenticate with Hugging Face using one of these methods:

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\item
  \textbf{Using \texttt{huggingface-cli} (recommended):}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\ExtensionTok{!pip}\NormalTok{ install }\AttributeTok{{-}q}\NormalTok{ huggingface\_hub}
\ExtensionTok{!huggingface{-}cli}\NormalTok{ login}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

  This will prompt you to enter your Hugging Face token. You can create
  a token at \url{https://huggingface.co/settings/tokens}.
\item
  \textbf{Using Google Colab Secrets:} Add your Hugging Face token as a
  secret named \texttt{HF\_TOKEN} in Colab (click the key icon in the
  left sidebar), then restart your session.
\item
  \textbf{Programmatic login:}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\ImportTok{from}\NormalTok{ huggingface\_hub }\ImportTok{import}\NormalTok{ login}
\NormalTok{login(token}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"your\_token\_here"}\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}
\end{enumerate}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\CommentTok{\# Find and print available Prithvi models in the model registry}
\BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{([model\_name }\ControlFlowTok{for}\NormalTok{ model\_name }\KeywordTok{in}\NormalTok{ BACKBONE\_REGISTRY }\ControlFlowTok{if} \StringTok{"terratorch\_prithvi"} \KeywordTok{in}\NormalTok{ model\_name])}

\CommentTok{\# Check that the model we want to use is in the registry}
\CommentTok{"terratorch\_prithvi\_eo\_v2\_300"} \KeywordTok{in}\NormalTok{ BACKBONE\_REGISTRY}

\CommentTok{\# instantiate our desired model}
\CommentTok{\# }\AlertTok{NOTE}\CommentTok{: the backbone registry prefix (e.g., \textasciigrave{}terratorch\textasciigrave{}) is optional}
\NormalTok{model }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ BACKBONE\_REGISTRY.build(}\StringTok{"prithvi\_eo\_v2\_300"}\NormalTok{, pretrained}\OperatorTok{=}\VariableTok{True}\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\subsection{Format Band Pixel Data for the
Model}\label{format-band-pixel-data-for-the-model}

Now we will transform the imagery stack into the format required by the
model. Prithvi uses short time series of four images, ``quadruplets,''
as input. Due to limited data, overlapping dates are used to create
quadruplets: the first spans dates 1 to 4, the second covers dates 2 to
5, and so forth.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{input\_size }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ [}\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{,size,size]}
\NormalTok{patch\_size }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ [}\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{,}\DecValTok{16}\NormalTok{,}\DecValTok{16}\NormalTok{]}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{chips }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ []}
\ControlFlowTok{for}\NormalTok{ i }\KeywordTok{in} \BuiltInTok{range}\NormalTok{(}\DecValTok{19}\NormalTok{):}
\NormalTok{    chips.append(stack.isel(time}\OperatorTok{=}\BuiltInTok{slice}\NormalTok{(i, i }\OperatorTok{+} \DecValTok{4}\NormalTok{)).values)}
\NormalTok{chips[}\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{].shape}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{chips }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ []}
\CommentTok{\# Calculate number of valid quadruplets (sequences of 4 timesteps)}
\NormalTok{num\_chips }\OperatorTok{=} \BuiltInTok{max}\NormalTok{(}\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{, stack.shape[}\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{] }\OperatorTok{{-}} \DecValTok{3}\NormalTok{)  }\CommentTok{\# stack.shape[0] is number of timesteps}
\BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(}\SpecialStringTok{f"Creating }\SpecialCharTok{\{}\NormalTok{num\_chips}\SpecialCharTok{\}}\SpecialStringTok{ chips from }\SpecialCharTok{\{}\NormalTok{stack}\SpecialCharTok{.}\NormalTok{shape[}\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{]}\SpecialCharTok{\}}\SpecialStringTok{ timesteps"}\NormalTok{)}

\ControlFlowTok{for}\NormalTok{ i }\KeywordTok{in} \BuiltInTok{range}\NormalTok{(num\_chips):}
\NormalTok{    chips.append(stack.isel(time}\OperatorTok{=}\BuiltInTok{slice}\NormalTok{(i, i }\OperatorTok{+} \DecValTok{4}\NormalTok{)).values)}
\NormalTok{chips[}\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{].shape}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\BuiltInTok{len}\NormalTok{(chips)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

We get \texttt{n} chips to represent the number combinations of temporal
sequences where the time series length is 4.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{chip\_time\_indices }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ [[i, i}\OperatorTok{+}\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{, i}\OperatorTok{+}\DecValTok{2}\NormalTok{, i}\OperatorTok{+}\DecValTok{3}\NormalTok{] }\ControlFlowTok{for}\NormalTok{ i }\KeywordTok{in} \BuiltInTok{range}\NormalTok{(num\_chips)]}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\BuiltInTok{len}\NormalTok{(chip\_time\_indices)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{chip\_time\_indices}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\subsection{Execute the Model}\label{execute-the-model}

Now we pass the prepared datacube to the model to generate one embedding
vector for each image.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{embeddings }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ []}
\NormalTok{start\_time }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ time.time()}

\CommentTok{\# Iterate over each chip (sequence of 4 timesteps)}
\ControlFlowTok{for}\NormalTok{ ts }\KeywordTok{in}\NormalTok{ chips:}
    \CommentTok{\# Rearrange tensor to match model input format: add batch dimension and put time in the middle}
\NormalTok{    ts }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ rearrange(ts, }\StringTok{"t c h w {-}\textgreater{} 1 c t h w"}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{    ts }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ ts.astype(np.float32)}
    \CommentTok{\# Only process chips with 4 time steps (quadruplets)}
    \ControlFlowTok{if}\NormalTok{ ts.shape[}\DecValTok{2}\NormalTok{] }\OperatorTok{==} \DecValTok{4}\NormalTok{:}
\NormalTok{        embedding }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ model.forward(torch.from\_numpy(ts)) }\CommentTok{\# Run model to get embeddings}
        \CommentTok{\# Extract the class token (represents a summary or global representation of the entire input sequence (here, 4 timesteps of the chip))}
        \CommentTok{\# \textasciigrave{}:\textasciigrave{} means all batches — here, size 1, \textasciigrave{}0\textasciigrave{} means the first token (the CLS token), \textasciigrave{}:\textasciigrave{} means all embedding dimensions}
        \CommentTok{\# so the shape of cls\_embedding before unraveling is (batch\_size, embedding\_dim), and if the batch size is 1, then after it is (embedding\_dim,)}
\NormalTok{        cls\_embedding }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ embedding[}\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{][:, }\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{, :].detach().cpu().numpy().ravel()}
\NormalTok{        embeddings.append(cls\_embedding)}
    \ControlFlowTok{else}\NormalTok{:}
        \BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(}\SpecialStringTok{f"Skipping chip with }\SpecialCharTok{\{}\NormalTok{ts}\SpecialCharTok{.}\NormalTok{shape[}\DecValTok{2}\NormalTok{]}\SpecialCharTok{\}}\SpecialStringTok{ time steps"}\NormalTok{)}

\NormalTok{embeddings }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ np.array(embeddings)}
\BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(}\SpecialStringTok{f"Generated }\SpecialCharTok{\{}\BuiltInTok{len}\NormalTok{(embeddings)}\SpecialCharTok{\}}\SpecialStringTok{ embeddings in }\SpecialCharTok{\{}\NormalTok{time}\SpecialCharTok{.}\NormalTok{time() }\OperatorTok{{-}}\NormalTok{ start\_time}\SpecialCharTok{:.1f\}}\SpecialStringTok{ seconds"}\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{embeddiwngs[}\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{].shape}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\BuiltInTok{len}\NormalTok{(embeddings)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\subsection{Analyze the Embeddings}\label{analyze-the-embeddings}

Now we will run a simple analysis to find any patterns in the model's
learned representations of the data. PCA is applied to reduce each
embedding to a single value. Embeddings, whether class or patch level,
from models like transformers are usually high-dimensional vectors
(e.g., 768, 1024 dims). PCA reduces this to fewer dimensions (e.g., 1 or
2) for easier analysis or visualization. By projecting embeddings into
2D with PCA, you can visualize how samples relate to each
other---whether they cluster by class, time, or some other property.
This is because PCA extracts principal components that capture the
largest variance in the embeddings, potentially removing noise or
irrelevant features and focusing on the most important aspects. As such,
lower-dimensional representations from PCA can be used as input features
for clustering, classification, or anomaly detection algorithms with
less computational cost and potentially better generalization.

The PCA-reduced embeddings, as you'll see, appear to be grouped into
four categories within PCA space: pre-flood images, during flood images,
cloudy images, and post-flood images.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\CommentTok{\# Define flood phase indices for visualization}
\CommentTok{\# These indices correspond to the temporal position of images in the stack}
\NormalTok{PRE\_FLOOD\_IDX }\OperatorTok{=} \BuiltInTok{slice}\NormalTok{(}\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{5}\NormalTok{)      }\CommentTok{\# Images before the flood}
\NormalTok{FLOOD\_IDX }\OperatorTok{=} \BuiltInTok{slice}\NormalTok{(}\DecValTok{5}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{9}\NormalTok{)          }\CommentTok{\# Images during active flooding}
\NormalTok{POST\_FLOOD\_IDX }\OperatorTok{=} \BuiltInTok{slice}\NormalTok{(}\DecValTok{9}\NormalTok{, }\VariableTok{None}\NormalTok{)  }\CommentTok{\# Images after flood waters receded}
\NormalTok{CLOUDY\_IDX }\OperatorTok{=} \DecValTok{7}                   \CommentTok{\# Index of cloudy image}

\NormalTok{pca }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ decomposition.PCA(n\_components}\OperatorTok{=}\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{pca\_result }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ pca.fit\_transform(embeddings)}

\NormalTok{plt.figure(figsize}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{(}\DecValTok{10}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{5}\NormalTok{))}
\NormalTok{plt.xticks(rotation}\OperatorTok{={-}}\DecValTok{45}\NormalTok{)}

\CommentTok{\# Plot points by flood phase}
\NormalTok{plt.scatter(stack.time[PRE\_FLOOD\_IDX], pca\_result[PRE\_FLOOD\_IDX], color}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"blue"}\NormalTok{, label}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"Pre{-}flood"}\NormalTok{, s}\OperatorTok{=}\DecValTok{80}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{plt.scatter(stack.time[FLOOD\_IDX], pca\_result[FLOOD\_IDX], color}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"red"}\NormalTok{, label}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"During flood"}\NormalTok{, s}\OperatorTok{=}\DecValTok{80}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{plt.scatter(stack.time[CLOUDY\_IDX], pca\_result[CLOUDY\_IDX], color}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"green"}\NormalTok{, label}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"Cloudy"}\NormalTok{, s}\OperatorTok{=}\DecValTok{80}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{plt.scatter(stack.time[POST\_FLOOD\_IDX][:num\_chips}\OperatorTok{{-}}\DecValTok{9}\NormalTok{], pca\_result[POST\_FLOOD\_IDX], color}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"orange"}\NormalTok{, label}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"Post{-}flood"}\NormalTok{, s}\OperatorTok{=}\DecValTok{80}\NormalTok{)}

\NormalTok{plt.xlabel(}\StringTok{"Date"}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{plt.ylabel(}\StringTok{"PCA Component 1"}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{plt.title(}\StringTok{"PCA Projection of Prithvi Embeddings Over Time"}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{plt.legend()}
\NormalTok{plt.tight\_layout()}
\NormalTok{plt.show()}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\subsection{Embeddings for Each Time
Step}\label{embeddings-for-each-time-step}

The model also generates embeddings for individual time steps. These
embeddings are extracted for each input quadruplet and visualized using
PCA, revealing similar clustering patterns.

The first version generates an embedding for each timesep in each
quadruplet, which encounters some redundancy as a given timestep is
inherently present in multiple quadruplets.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{long\_embeddings\_quadruplets }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ []}

\CommentTok{\# Iterate over each chip (sequence of 4 timesteps)}
\ControlFlowTok{for}\NormalTok{ ts }\KeywordTok{in}\NormalTok{ chips:}
    \CommentTok{\# Rearrange tensor to match model input format: add batch dimension and put time in the middle}
\NormalTok{    ts }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ rearrange(ts, }\StringTok{"t c h w {-}\textgreater{} 1 c t h w"}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{    ts }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ ts.astype(np.float32)}
    \CommentTok{\# Only process chips with 4 time steps (quadruplets)}
    \ControlFlowTok{if}\NormalTok{ ts.shape[}\DecValTok{2}\NormalTok{] }\OperatorTok{==} \DecValTok{4}\NormalTok{:}
\NormalTok{        embedding }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ model.forward(torch.from\_numpy(ts)) }\CommentTok{\# Run model to get embeddings}
        \CommentTok{\# Extract class token (embedding vector for the whole chip (4 timesteps combined))}
\NormalTok{        cls\_embedding }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ embedding[}\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{][:, }\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{, :].detach().cpu().numpy().ravel()}
        \CommentTok{\# Reshape to (t, n, d): time, patch tokens, embedding dim}
\NormalTok{        embedding }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ rearrange(embedding[}\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{][:, }\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{:, :], }\StringTok{"1 (t n) d {-}\textgreater{} 1 t n d"}\NormalTok{, t}\OperatorTok{=}\DecValTok{4}\NormalTok{)[}\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{]}
        \CommentTok{\# Average patch tokens per timestep: shape becomes (t, d)}
        \CommentTok{\# gives us one averaged patch embedding per timestep}
\NormalTok{        embedding }\OperatorTok{=} \BuiltInTok{reduce}\NormalTok{(embedding, }\StringTok{"t n d {-}\textgreater{} t d"}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{"mean"}\NormalTok{).detach().numpy()}
        \CommentTok{\# Store the embedding quadruplet}
\NormalTok{        t0, t1, t2, t3 }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ embedding}
\NormalTok{        long\_embeddings\_quadruplets.extend([t0] }\OperatorTok{+}\NormalTok{ [t1] }\OperatorTok{+}\NormalTok{ [t2] }\OperatorTok{+}\NormalTok{ [t3])}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\BuiltInTok{len}\NormalTok{(long\_embeddings\_quadruplets)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

This second version stores only one embedding for a given timestep
(appends the embedding based on its time index if that time index has
not already been added).

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{long\_embeddings\_dict }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ \{\}}

\CommentTok{\# Iterate over each chip (sequence of 4 timesteps) and its index}
\ControlFlowTok{for}\NormalTok{ i, ts }\KeywordTok{in} \BuiltInTok{enumerate}\NormalTok{(chips):}
\NormalTok{    time\_ids }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ chip\_time\_indices[i]  }\CommentTok{\# Global timestep indices for this chip, e.g., [0, 1, 2, 3]}
    \CommentTok{\# Rearrange tensor to match model input format: add batch dimension and put time in the middle}
\NormalTok{    ts }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ rearrange(ts, }\StringTok{"t c h w {-}\textgreater{} 1 c t h w"}\NormalTok{).astype(np.float32)}

    \CommentTok{\# Only process chips with 4 time steps (quadruplets)}
    \ControlFlowTok{if}\NormalTok{ ts.shape[}\DecValTok{2}\NormalTok{] }\OperatorTok{==} \DecValTok{4}\NormalTok{:}
        \ControlFlowTok{with}\NormalTok{ torch.no\_grad():}
\NormalTok{            embedding }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ model.forward(torch.from\_numpy(ts)) }\CommentTok{\# Run model to get embeddings}

        \CommentTok{\# Extract CLS token, reshape to (t, n, d): time, patch tokens, embedding dim}
\NormalTok{        embedding }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ rearrange(embedding[}\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{][:, }\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{:, :], }\StringTok{"1 (t n) d {-}\textgreater{} 1 t n d"}\NormalTok{, t}\OperatorTok{=}\DecValTok{4}\NormalTok{)[}\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{]}
        \CommentTok{\# Average patch tokens per timestep: shape becomes (t, d)}
        \CommentTok{\# gives us one averaged patch embedding per timestep}
\NormalTok{        embedding }\OperatorTok{=} \BuiltInTok{reduce}\NormalTok{(embedding, }\StringTok{"t n d {-}\textgreater{} t d"}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{"mean"}\NormalTok{).detach().numpy()}

        \CommentTok{\# Store each timestep\textquotesingle{}s embedding using the original (global) time index}
        \ControlFlowTok{for}\NormalTok{ j, t\_idx }\KeywordTok{in} \BuiltInTok{enumerate}\NormalTok{(time\_ids):}
            \ControlFlowTok{if}\NormalTok{ t\_idx }\KeywordTok{not} \KeywordTok{in}\NormalTok{ long\_embeddings\_dict:}
\NormalTok{                long\_embeddings\_dict[t\_idx] }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ embedding[j]}

\CommentTok{\# Reconstruct a list of embeddings ordered by all 22 possible timesteps (if available)}
\NormalTok{all\_timesteps }\OperatorTok{=} \BuiltInTok{list}\NormalTok{(}\BuiltInTok{range}\NormalTok{(}\DecValTok{22}\NormalTok{))}
\NormalTok{long\_embeddings }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ [long\_embeddings\_dict[t] }\ControlFlowTok{for}\NormalTok{ t }\KeywordTok{in}\NormalTok{ all\_timesteps }\ControlFlowTok{if}\NormalTok{ t }\KeywordTok{in}\NormalTok{ long\_embeddings\_dict]}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\BuiltInTok{len}\NormalTok{(long\_embeddings)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\CommentTok{\# Run PCA}
\NormalTok{pca }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ decomposition.PCA(n\_components}\OperatorTok{=}\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{pca\_result }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ pca.fit\_transform(long\_embeddings)}

\NormalTok{plt.figure(figsize}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{(}\DecValTok{10}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{5}\NormalTok{))}
\NormalTok{plt.xticks(rotation}\OperatorTok{={-}}\DecValTok{45}\NormalTok{)}

\CommentTok{\# Plot points by flood phase}
\NormalTok{plt.scatter(stack.time[PRE\_FLOOD\_IDX], pca\_result[PRE\_FLOOD\_IDX], color}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"blue"}\NormalTok{, label}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"Pre{-}flood"}\NormalTok{, s}\OperatorTok{=}\DecValTok{80}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{plt.scatter(stack.time[FLOOD\_IDX], pca\_result[FLOOD\_IDX], color}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"red"}\NormalTok{, label}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"During flood"}\NormalTok{, s}\OperatorTok{=}\DecValTok{80}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{plt.scatter(stack.time[CLOUDY\_IDX], pca\_result[CLOUDY\_IDX], color}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"green"}\NormalTok{, label}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"Cloudy"}\NormalTok{, s}\OperatorTok{=}\DecValTok{80}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{plt.scatter(stack.time[POST\_FLOOD\_IDX], pca\_result[POST\_FLOOD\_IDX], color}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"orange"}\NormalTok{, label}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"Post{-}flood"}\NormalTok{, s}\OperatorTok{=}\DecValTok{80}\NormalTok{)}

\NormalTok{plt.xlabel(}\StringTok{"Date"}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{plt.ylabel(}\StringTok{"PCA Component 1"}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{plt.title(}\StringTok{"PCA Projection of Per{-}Timestep Embeddings Over Time"}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{plt.legend()}
\NormalTok{plt.tight\_layout()}
\NormalTok{plt.show()}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\subsubsection{Visualizing Flood Patterns with
t-SNE}\label{visualizing-flood-patterns-with-t-sne}

While PCA provides a linear dimensionality reduction,
\href{https://scikit-learn.org/stable/modules/generated/sklearn.manifold.TSNE.html}{t-SNE
(t-distributed Stochastic Neighbor Embedding)} captures non-linear
relationships in the embedding space. t-SNE is particularly effective at
revealing clustering patterns by preserving local neighborhood
structure---making it well-suited for visualizing how the model
distinguishes between different flood states (see Figure 5).

In the context of our flood analysis, t-SNE helps us understand whether
the foundation model's embeddings naturally separate pre-flood
conditions (e.g.~normal agricultural land, urban areas and water bodies)
from active flooding (e.g.~inundated areas, turbid water) and post-flood
recovery (e.g.~receding waters, sediment deposits). If the model has
learned meaningful representations of these hydrological states, we
expect to see distinct clusters corresponding to each phase of the flood
event.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{long\_embeddings\_array }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ np.array(long\_embeddings)}

\NormalTok{tsne }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ TSNE(n\_components}\OperatorTok{=}\DecValTok{2}\NormalTok{, random\_state}\OperatorTok{=}\DecValTok{42}\NormalTok{, perplexity}\OperatorTok{=}\BuiltInTok{min}\NormalTok{(}\DecValTok{5}\NormalTok{, }\BuiltInTok{len}\NormalTok{(long\_embeddings)}\OperatorTok{{-}}\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{))}
\NormalTok{tsne\_result }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ tsne.fit\_transform(long\_embeddings\_array)}

\NormalTok{plt.figure(figsize}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{(}\DecValTok{8}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{6}\NormalTok{))}
\NormalTok{plt.scatter(tsne\_result[PRE\_FLOOD\_IDX, }\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{], tsne\_result[PRE\_FLOOD\_IDX, }\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{], color}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}blue\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, label}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}Pre{-}flood\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, s}\OperatorTok{=}\DecValTok{100}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{plt.scatter(tsne\_result[FLOOD\_IDX, }\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{], tsne\_result[FLOOD\_IDX, }\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{], color}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}red\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, label}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}During flood\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, s}\OperatorTok{=}\DecValTok{100}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{plt.scatter(tsne\_result[CLOUDY\_IDX, }\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{], tsne\_result[CLOUDY\_IDX, }\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{], color}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}green\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, label}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}Cloudy\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, s}\OperatorTok{=}\DecValTok{100}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{plt.scatter(tsne\_result[POST\_FLOOD\_IDX, }\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{], tsne\_result[POST\_FLOOD\_IDX, }\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{], color}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}orange\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, label}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}Post{-}flood\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, s}\OperatorTok{=}\DecValTok{100}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{plt.xlabel(}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}t{-}SNE Dimension 1\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{plt.ylabel(}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}t{-}SNE Dimension 2\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{plt.title(}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}t{-}SNE Projection of Prithvi Embeddings\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{plt.legend()}
\NormalTok{plt.tight\_layout()}
\NormalTok{plt.show()}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\subsubsection{Interpretation: Prithvi
Embeddings}\label{interpretation-prithvi-embeddings}

The PCA and t-SNE visualizations above reveal that Prithvi's embeddings
capture meaningful distinctions between flood phases. Pre-flood images
(blue) cluster separately from active flooding images (red), indicating
the model has learned to differentiate normal land conditions from
storm-affected areas. The post-flood images (orange/yellow) form their
own cluster. The cloudy image (green) appears as an outlier,
demonstrating that the model's embeddings are also sensitive to
atmospheric interference---an important consideration for operational
flood mapping where cloud contamination is common.

\subsection{Train a Model using the Embeddings as
Features}\label{train-a-model-using-the-embeddings-as-features}

Now we will train a classifier head for detecting flood events on top of
the embeddings. Our dataset is tiny (22 samples), so we will choose a
suitable method: Support Vector Machine. An SVM is a supervised machine
learning model that tries to find the best boundary (or boundaries)
between different classes of data in a feature space. At this point,
we're treating the EOFM as a feature extractor (fixed), and only
training a small classifier (the SVM) on top. Given that, SVM fits well
--- it learns fast and doesn't overfit as easily as deeper classifiers.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\CommentTok{\# Label the images we downloaded}
\CommentTok{\# 0 = Cloud}
\CommentTok{\# 1 = Pre{-}flood}
\CommentTok{\# 2 = During{-}flood}
\CommentTok{\# 3 = Post{-}flood}

\NormalTok{labels }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ np.array([}\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{2}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{2}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{2}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{3}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{3}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{3}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{3}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{3}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{3}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{3}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{3}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{3}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{3}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{3}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{3}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{3}\NormalTok{])}

\CommentTok{\# Split into fit and test manually, with an effort to have all 3 classes in both sets}
\CommentTok{\# (since there is only one cloudy sample, we use it for training only)}
\NormalTok{fit }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ [}\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{2}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{4}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{6}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{7}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{8}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{10}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{12}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{14}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{16}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{18}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{20}\NormalTok{]}
\NormalTok{test }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ [}\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{3}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{5}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{9}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{11}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{13}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{15}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{17}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{19}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{21}\NormalTok{]}

\CommentTok{\# Train a support vector machine model}
\NormalTok{long\_embeddings }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ np.array(long\_embeddings)}
\NormalTok{clf }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ svm.SVC()}
\NormalTok{clf.fit(long\_embeddings[fit], labels[fit])}

\CommentTok{\# Predict classes on test set}
\NormalTok{prediction }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ clf.predict(long\_embeddings[test])}

\CommentTok{\# Match for SVM}
\NormalTok{match }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ np.}\BuiltInTok{sum}\NormalTok{(labels[test] }\OperatorTok{==}\NormalTok{ prediction)}
\BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(}\SpecialStringTok{f"Matched }\SpecialCharTok{\{}\NormalTok{match}\SpecialCharTok{\}}\SpecialStringTok{ out of }\SpecialCharTok{\{}\BuiltInTok{len}\NormalTok{(test)}\SpecialCharTok{\}}\SpecialStringTok{ correctly"}\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\section{Clay}\label{clay}

The Clay Foundation model is another open source EOFM. It also utilizes
a \textbf{Vision Transformer (ViT)} architecture with a \textbf{masked
autoencoder} approach. The encoder of the model consists of several
layers, each with multiple attention heads, allowing it to capture
complex spatial and temporal relationships in the data. The
self-attention mechanism enables it to understand both local and global
features within satellite imagery. The decoder is structured similarly
but with fewer layers and attention heads, and it is optimized for
efficient reconstruction during the pre-training phase. The architecture
uses an MLP (Multi-Layer Perceptron) layer for feature extraction.

The model is trained on a large dataset that combines multi-modal
geospatial data from Landsat 8 and 9, Sentinel-2 L2A, Sentinel-1 RTC,
NAIP and LINZ. A sampling strategy carefully curated to cover a variety
of land types was implemented, ensuring diverse geographical and
seasonal representation.

The architecture and data inputs make Clay highly flexible.

For more details, you can visit the official
\href{https://madewithclay.org}{Clay Foundation website} or the
\href{https://clay-foundation.github.io/model/index.html}{documentation
website}.

Let's get the necessary code for Clay.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\OperatorTok{!}\NormalTok{mkdir clay}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{cd clay}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\OperatorTok{!}\NormalTok{git clone }\OperatorTok{{-}{-}}\NormalTok{depth}\OperatorTok{=}\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{ https:}\OperatorTok{//}\NormalTok{github.com}\OperatorTok{/}\NormalTok{Clay}\OperatorTok{{-}}\NormalTok{foundation}\OperatorTok{/}\NormalTok{model}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{cd model}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{ls}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\ImportTok{import}\NormalTok{ sys}
\NormalTok{sys.path.append(}\StringTok{"./claymodel/"}\NormalTok{)}
\ImportTok{from}\NormalTok{ claymodel.module }\ImportTok{import}\NormalTok{ ClayMAEModule}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

The tile size for Prithvi was 256. For Clay, it is 224, so we will need
to re-create our stacked dataset using that value.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\CommentTok{\# Create bounds in projection}
\NormalTok{size }\OperatorTok{=} \DecValTok{224}
\NormalTok{gsd }\OperatorTok{=} \DecValTok{30}
\NormalTok{bounds }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ (}
\NormalTok{    coords[}\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{] }\OperatorTok{{-}}\NormalTok{ (size }\OperatorTok{*}\NormalTok{ gsd) }\OperatorTok{//} \DecValTok{2}\NormalTok{,}
\NormalTok{    coords[}\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{] }\OperatorTok{{-}}\NormalTok{ (size }\OperatorTok{*}\NormalTok{ gsd) }\OperatorTok{//} \DecValTok{2}\NormalTok{,}
\NormalTok{    coords[}\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{] }\OperatorTok{+}\NormalTok{ (size }\OperatorTok{*}\NormalTok{ gsd) }\OperatorTok{//} \DecValTok{2}\NormalTok{,}
\NormalTok{    coords[}\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{] }\OperatorTok{+}\NormalTok{ (size }\OperatorTok{*}\NormalTok{ gsd) }\OperatorTok{//} \DecValTok{2}\NormalTok{,}
\NormalTok{)}

\CommentTok{\# Retrieve the pixel values, for the bounding box in}
\CommentTok{\# the target projection. In this example we use the}
\CommentTok{\# RGB, NIR and SWIR bands.}
\NormalTok{stack }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ stackstac.stack(}
\NormalTok{    items,}
\NormalTok{    bounds}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{bounds,}
\NormalTok{    snap\_bounds}\OperatorTok{=}\VariableTok{False}\NormalTok{,}
\NormalTok{    epsg}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{epsg,}
\NormalTok{    resolution}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{gsd,}
\NormalTok{    dtype}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"float64"}\NormalTok{,}
\NormalTok{    rescale}\OperatorTok{=}\VariableTok{False}\NormalTok{,}
\NormalTok{    fill\_value}\OperatorTok{=}\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{,}
\NormalTok{    assets}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{[}\StringTok{"B02"}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{"B03"}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{"B04"}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{"B05"}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{"B06"}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{"B07"}\NormalTok{],}
\NormalTok{    resampling}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{Resampling.nearest,}
\NormalTok{)}

\BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(}\SpecialStringTok{f"Working with stack of size }\SpecialCharTok{\{}\NormalTok{stack}\SpecialCharTok{.}\NormalTok{shape}\SpecialCharTok{\}}\SpecialStringTok{"}\NormalTok{)}

\NormalTok{stack }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ stack.compute()}

\NormalTok{stack}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\subsection{Load the Model}\label{load-the-model-1}

We now have the data to analyse, let's load the model. But first, we
need to rename the bands to match what's expected in the config file.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{stack.band.values}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\ControlFlowTok{if} \StringTok{"band"} \KeywordTok{in}\NormalTok{ stack.coords:}
\NormalTok{    stack }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ stack.assign\_coords(band}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{[}\StringTok{"red"} \ControlFlowTok{if}\NormalTok{ b }\OperatorTok{==} \StringTok{"B04"} \ControlFlowTok{else}\NormalTok{ b }\ControlFlowTok{for}\NormalTok{ b }\KeywordTok{in}\NormalTok{ stack.band.values])}
\NormalTok{    stack }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ stack.assign\_coords(band}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{[}\StringTok{"green"} \ControlFlowTok{if}\NormalTok{ b }\OperatorTok{==} \StringTok{"B03"} \ControlFlowTok{else}\NormalTok{ b }\ControlFlowTok{for}\NormalTok{ b }\KeywordTok{in}\NormalTok{ stack.band.values])}
\NormalTok{    stack }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ stack.assign\_coords(band}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{[}\StringTok{"blue"} \ControlFlowTok{if}\NormalTok{ b }\OperatorTok{==} \StringTok{"B02"} \ControlFlowTok{else}\NormalTok{ b }\ControlFlowTok{for}\NormalTok{ b }\KeywordTok{in}\NormalTok{ stack.band.values])}
\NormalTok{    stack }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ stack.assign\_coords(band}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{[}\StringTok{"nir"} \ControlFlowTok{if}\NormalTok{ b }\OperatorTok{==} \StringTok{"B05"} \ControlFlowTok{else}\NormalTok{ b }\ControlFlowTok{for}\NormalTok{ b }\KeywordTok{in}\NormalTok{ stack.band.values])}
\NormalTok{    stack }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ stack.assign\_coords(band}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{[}\StringTok{"swir16"} \ControlFlowTok{if}\NormalTok{ b }\OperatorTok{==} \StringTok{"B06"} \ControlFlowTok{else}\NormalTok{ b }\ControlFlowTok{for}\NormalTok{ b }\KeywordTok{in}\NormalTok{ stack.band.values])}
\NormalTok{    stack }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ stack.assign\_coords(band}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{[}\StringTok{"swir22"} \ControlFlowTok{if}\NormalTok{ b }\OperatorTok{==} \StringTok{"B07"} \ControlFlowTok{else}\NormalTok{ b }\ControlFlowTok{for}\NormalTok{ b }\KeywordTok{in}\NormalTok{ stack.band.values])}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

Again, here you have the option to load the model the following way or
with the HuggingFace library.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{device }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ torch.device(}\StringTok{"cuda"}\NormalTok{) }\ControlFlowTok{if}\NormalTok{ torch.cuda.is\_available() }\ControlFlowTok{else}\NormalTok{ torch.device(}\StringTok{"cpu"}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{ckpt }\OperatorTok{=} \StringTok{"https://huggingface.co/made{-}with{-}clay/Clay/resolve/main/v1.5/clay{-}v1.5.ckpt"}
\NormalTok{torch.set\_default\_device(device)}

\NormalTok{model }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ ClayMAEModule.load\_from\_checkpoint(}
\NormalTok{    ckpt,}
\NormalTok{    model\_size}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"large"}\NormalTok{,}
\NormalTok{    metadata\_path}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"./configs/metadata.yaml"}\NormalTok{,}
\NormalTok{    mask\_ratio}\OperatorTok{=}\FloatTok{0.0}\NormalTok{,}
\NormalTok{    shuffle}\OperatorTok{=}\VariableTok{False}\NormalTok{,}
\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{model.}\BuiltInTok{eval}\NormalTok{()}

\NormalTok{model }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ model.to(device)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\subsection{Prepare Band Metadata for Model
Input}\label{prepare-band-metadata-for-model-input}

This step is the most technical so far. It involves processing the
imagery stack and converting its data into the format required by the
model. This includes normalizing the latitude/longitude and the
imagery's date values.

The Clay model is designed to accept any combination of bands in any
order, even from different platforms. However, the model requires
metadata about each band, including its central wavelength and
normalization parameters. This information allows the model to
standardize the data and correctly interpret each band based on its
wavelength.

For Sentinel-2, we can extract these details from the model's metadata
file. For other platforms, a custom approach may be needed to provide
this information. For simplicity here, we utilize the Sentinel-2
parameters for our HLS imagery.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\CommentTok{\# Extract mean, std, and wavelengths from metadata}
\NormalTok{platform }\OperatorTok{=} \StringTok{"sentinel{-}2{-}l2a"}
\NormalTok{metadata }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ Box(yaml.safe\_load(}\BuiltInTok{open}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"configs/metadata.yaml"}\NormalTok{)))}
\NormalTok{mean }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ []}
\NormalTok{std }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ []}
\NormalTok{waves }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ []}
\CommentTok{\# Use the band names to get the correct values in the correct order.}
\ControlFlowTok{for}\NormalTok{ band }\KeywordTok{in}\NormalTok{ stack.band:}
\NormalTok{    mean.append(metadata[platform].bands.mean[}\BuiltInTok{str}\NormalTok{(band.values)])}
\NormalTok{    std.append(metadata[platform].bands.std[}\BuiltInTok{str}\NormalTok{(band.values)])}
\NormalTok{    waves.append(metadata[platform].bands.wavelength[}\BuiltInTok{str}\NormalTok{(band.values)])}

\CommentTok{\# Prepare the normalization transform function using the mean and std values.}
\NormalTok{transform }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ v2.Compose(}
\NormalTok{    [}
\NormalTok{        v2.Normalize(mean}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{mean, std}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{std),}
\NormalTok{    ]}
\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\subsection{Convert Band Pixel Data to Model
Format}\label{convert-band-pixel-data-to-model-format}

This step involves transforming the imagery stack into the format
required by the model. It includes normalizing the latitude/longitude
and the imagery's date values.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\CommentTok{\# Prep datetimes embedding using a normalization function from the model code.}
\KeywordTok{def}\NormalTok{ normalize\_timestamp(date):}
\NormalTok{    week }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ date.isocalendar().week }\OperatorTok{*} \DecValTok{2} \OperatorTok{*}\NormalTok{ np.pi }\OperatorTok{/} \DecValTok{52}
\NormalTok{    hour }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ date.hour }\OperatorTok{*} \DecValTok{2} \OperatorTok{*}\NormalTok{ np.pi }\OperatorTok{/} \DecValTok{24}

    \ControlFlowTok{return}\NormalTok{ (math.sin(week), math.cos(week)), (math.sin(hour), math.cos(hour))}


\NormalTok{datetimes }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ stack.time.values.astype(}\StringTok{"datetime64[s]"}\NormalTok{).tolist()}
\NormalTok{times }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ [normalize\_timestamp(dat) }\ControlFlowTok{for}\NormalTok{ dat }\KeywordTok{in}\NormalTok{ datetimes]}
\NormalTok{week\_norm }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ [dat[}\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{] }\ControlFlowTok{for}\NormalTok{ dat }\KeywordTok{in}\NormalTok{ times]}
\NormalTok{hour\_norm }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ [dat[}\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{] }\ControlFlowTok{for}\NormalTok{ dat }\KeywordTok{in}\NormalTok{ times]}


\CommentTok{\# Prep lat/lon embedding using a normalization function from the model code.}
\KeywordTok{def}\NormalTok{ normalize\_latlon(lat, lon):}
\NormalTok{    lat }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ lat }\OperatorTok{*}\NormalTok{ np.pi }\OperatorTok{/} \DecValTok{180}
\NormalTok{    lon }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ lon }\OperatorTok{*}\NormalTok{ np.pi }\OperatorTok{/} \DecValTok{180}

    \ControlFlowTok{return}\NormalTok{ (math.sin(lat), math.cos(lat)), (math.sin(lon), math.cos(lon))}


\NormalTok{latlons }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ [normalize\_latlon(lat, lon)] }\OperatorTok{*} \BuiltInTok{len}\NormalTok{(times)}
\NormalTok{lat\_norm }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ [dat[}\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{] }\ControlFlowTok{for}\NormalTok{ dat }\KeywordTok{in}\NormalTok{ latlons]}
\NormalTok{lon\_norm }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ [dat[}\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{] }\ControlFlowTok{for}\NormalTok{ dat }\KeywordTok{in}\NormalTok{ latlons]}

\CommentTok{\# Normalize pixels}
\NormalTok{pixels }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ torch.from\_numpy(stack.data.astype(np.float32))}
\NormalTok{pixels }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ transform(pixels)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\subsection{Combine Metadata and
Pixels}\label{combine-metadata-and-pixels}

All required inputs, including metadata and transformed pixel values,
are compiled into a single dictionary.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\CommentTok{\# Prepare additional information}
\NormalTok{datacube }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ \{}
    \StringTok{"platform"}\NormalTok{: platform,}
    \StringTok{"time"}\NormalTok{: torch.tensor(}
\NormalTok{        np.hstack((week\_norm, hour\_norm)),}
\NormalTok{        dtype}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{torch.float32,}
\NormalTok{        device}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{device,}
\NormalTok{    ),}
    \StringTok{"latlon"}\NormalTok{: torch.tensor(}
\NormalTok{        np.hstack((lat\_norm, lon\_norm)), dtype}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{torch.float32, device}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{device}
\NormalTok{    ),}
    \StringTok{"pixels"}\NormalTok{: pixels.to(device),}
    \StringTok{"gsd"}\NormalTok{: torch.tensor(stack.rio.resolution()[}\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{], device}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{device),}
    \StringTok{"waves"}\NormalTok{: torch.tensor(waves, device}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{device),}
\NormalTok{\}}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\subsection{Execute the Model}\label{execute-the-model-1}

The datacube is passed to the model, which generates one embedding
vector per image.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\ImportTok{import}\NormalTok{ time}
\NormalTok{start\_time }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ time.time()}

\ControlFlowTok{with}\NormalTok{ torch.no\_grad():}
\NormalTok{    unmsk\_patch, unmsk\_idx, msk\_idx, msk\_matrix }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ model.model.encoder(datacube)}

\CommentTok{\# The first embedding is the class token, which is the}
\CommentTok{\# overall single embedding. We extract that for PCA below.}
\NormalTok{embeddings }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ unmsk\_patch[:, }\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{, :].cpu().numpy()}

\BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(}\SpecialStringTok{f"Generated }\SpecialCharTok{\{}\BuiltInTok{len}\NormalTok{(embeddings)}\SpecialCharTok{\}}\SpecialStringTok{ embeddings in }\SpecialCharTok{\{}\NormalTok{time}\SpecialCharTok{.}\NormalTok{time() }\OperatorTok{{-}}\NormalTok{ start\_time}\SpecialCharTok{:.1f\}}\SpecialStringTok{ seconds"}\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\BuiltInTok{len}\NormalTok{(embeddings)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\subsection{Analyze the Embeddings}\label{analyze-the-embeddings-1}

A straightforward way to analyze the embeddings is by reducing each to a
single value using Principal Component Analysis (PCA). This involves
fitting a PCA model to the embeddings and performing dimensionality
reduction. The resulting PCA space reveals three distinct groups:
earlier images, cloudy images, and post-flood images, each occupying a
different range within the PCA space.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\CommentTok{\# Run PCA}
\NormalTok{pca }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ decomposition.PCA(n\_components}\OperatorTok{=}\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{pca\_result }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ pca.fit\_transform(embeddings)}

\NormalTok{plt.figure(figsize}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{(}\DecValTok{10}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{5}\NormalTok{))}
\NormalTok{plt.xticks(rotation}\OperatorTok{={-}}\DecValTok{45}\NormalTok{)}

\CommentTok{\# Plot points by flood phase}
\NormalTok{plt.scatter(stack.time[PRE\_FLOOD\_IDX], pca\_result[PRE\_FLOOD\_IDX], color}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"blue"}\NormalTok{, label}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"Pre{-}flood"}\NormalTok{, s}\OperatorTok{=}\DecValTok{80}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{plt.scatter(stack.time[FLOOD\_IDX], pca\_result[FLOOD\_IDX], color}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"red"}\NormalTok{, label}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"During flood"}\NormalTok{, s}\OperatorTok{=}\DecValTok{80}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{plt.scatter(stack.time[CLOUDY\_IDX], pca\_result[CLOUDY\_IDX], color}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"green"}\NormalTok{, label}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"Cloudy"}\NormalTok{, s}\OperatorTok{=}\DecValTok{80}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{plt.scatter(stack.time[POST\_FLOOD\_IDX], pca\_result[POST\_FLOOD\_IDX], color}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"orange"}\NormalTok{, label}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"Post{-}flood"}\NormalTok{, s}\OperatorTok{=}\DecValTok{80}\NormalTok{)}

\NormalTok{plt.xlabel(}\StringTok{"Date"}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{plt.ylabel(}\StringTok{"PCA Component 1"}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{plt.title(}\StringTok{"PCA Projection of Clay Embeddings Over Time"}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{plt.legend()}
\NormalTok{plt.tight\_layout()}
\NormalTok{plt.show()}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{tsne }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ TSNE(n\_components}\OperatorTok{=}\DecValTok{2}\NormalTok{, random\_state}\OperatorTok{=}\DecValTok{42}\NormalTok{, perplexity}\OperatorTok{=}\BuiltInTok{min}\NormalTok{(}\DecValTok{5}\NormalTok{, }\BuiltInTok{len}\NormalTok{(embeddings)}\OperatorTok{{-}}\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{))}
\NormalTok{tsne\_result }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ tsne.fit\_transform(embeddings)}

\NormalTok{plt.figure(figsize}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{(}\DecValTok{8}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{6}\NormalTok{))}
\NormalTok{plt.scatter(tsne\_result[PRE\_FLOOD\_IDX, }\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{], tsne\_result[PRE\_FLOOD\_IDX, }\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{], color}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}blue\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, label}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}Pre{-}flood\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, s}\OperatorTok{=}\DecValTok{100}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{plt.scatter(tsne\_result[FLOOD\_IDX, }\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{], tsne\_result[FLOOD\_IDX, }\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{], color}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}red\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, label}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}During flood\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, s}\OperatorTok{=}\DecValTok{100}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{plt.scatter(tsne\_result[CLOUDY\_IDX, }\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{], tsne\_result[CLOUDY\_IDX, }\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{], color}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}green\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, label}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}Cloudy\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, s}\OperatorTok{=}\DecValTok{100}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{plt.scatter(tsne\_result[POST\_FLOOD\_IDX, }\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{], tsne\_result[POST\_FLOOD\_IDX, }\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{], color}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}orange\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, label}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}Post{-}flood\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, s}\OperatorTok{=}\DecValTok{100}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{plt.xlabel(}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}t{-}SNE Dimension 1\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{plt.ylabel(}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}t{-}SNE Dimension 2\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{plt.title(}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}t{-}SNE Projection of Clay Embeddings\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{plt.legend()}
\NormalTok{plt.tight\_layout()}
\NormalTok{plt.show()}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\subsubsection{Interpretation: Clay
Embeddings}\label{interpretation-clay-embeddings}

Similar to Prithvi, Clay's embeddings show clear separation between
flood phases in both PCA and t-SNE projections. The model successfully
distinguishes pre-flood conditions from active flooding and post-flood
recovery states. Notably, Clay processes each timestep independently
(unlike Prithvi's temporal quadruplets), yet still captures the flood
signal effectively. This suggests that the spectral and spatial patterns
of flooding are sufficiently distinctive that even single-image
embeddings can support flood detection. The consistency between Prithvi
and Clay results provides confidence that the observed patterns reflect
genuine flood signatures rather than model-specific artifacts.

\subsection{Train a Model using the Embeddings as
Features}\label{train-a-model-using-the-embeddings-as-features-1}

Finally, a classifier is again trained on the embeddings to identify
flood events.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\CommentTok{\# Label the images we downloaded}
\CommentTok{\# 0 = Cloud}
\CommentTok{\# 1 = Pre{-}flood}

\CommentTok{\# 2 = During{-}flood}
\CommentTok{\# 3 = Post{-}flood}

\NormalTok{labels }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ np.array([}\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{2}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{2}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{2}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{3}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{3}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{3}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{3}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{3}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{3}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{3}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{3}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{3}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{3}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{3}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{3}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{3}\NormalTok{])}

\CommentTok{\# Split into fit and test manually, with an effort to have all 3 classes in both sets}
\CommentTok{\# (since there is only one cloudy sample, we use it for training only)}
\NormalTok{fit }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ [}\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{2}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{4}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{6}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{7}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{8}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{10}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{12}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{14}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{16}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{18}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{20}\NormalTok{]}
\NormalTok{test }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ [}\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{3}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{5}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{9}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{11}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{13}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{15}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{17}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{19}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{21}\NormalTok{]}

\CommentTok{\# Train a Support Vector Machine model}
\NormalTok{clf }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ svm.SVC()}
\NormalTok{clf.fit(embeddings[fit], labels[fit])}

\CommentTok{\# Predict classes on test set}
\NormalTok{prediction }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ clf.predict(embeddings[test])}

\CommentTok{\# Perfect match for SVM}
\NormalTok{match }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ np.}\BuiltInTok{sum}\NormalTok{(labels[test] }\OperatorTok{==}\NormalTok{ prediction)}
\BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(}\SpecialStringTok{f"Matched }\SpecialCharTok{\{}\NormalTok{match}\SpecialCharTok{\}}\SpecialStringTok{ out of }\SpecialCharTok{\{}\BuiltInTok{len}\NormalTok{(test)}\SpecialCharTok{\}}\SpecialStringTok{ correctly"}\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{labels[test]}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{prediction}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\subsection{Change Detection Heatmap (Pre- vs.~Post-Flood
Distances)}\label{change-detection-heatmap-pre--vs.-post-flood-distances}

We can generate a heatmap to visualize pairwise distances between
embeddings from pre-flood and post-flood periods. Each cell's value
represents the distance between a specific pre-flood and post-flood
embedding.

We will also compute the average distance. A high average distance
suggests that the model identifies significant differences between pre-
and post-flood conditions. This could reflect meaningful changes in the
landscape.

As for the heatmap, pattern consistency is key to look for. Uniformity
in the heatmap (similar distances across all pairs) indicates a
consistent model response to changes. Non-uniformity might suggest
localized variations. Smaller distances between certain pairs might
highlight areas unaffected by the event or areas with similarities
across both periods.

Notice that the row impacted by the greatest distance includes the
cloudy image at index 8. This makes sense as the cloudy image will bear
greater dissimilarity to the other embeddings, both pre- and post-flood.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\CommentTok{\# Embeddings from pre{-} and post{-}flood}
\NormalTok{pre\_event\_embeddings }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ embeddings[}\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{:}\DecValTok{5}\NormalTok{]}
\NormalTok{post\_event\_embeddings }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ embeddings[}\DecValTok{9}\NormalTok{:]}

\CommentTok{\# Calculate pairwise distances between pre{-} and post{-}event embeddings}
\CommentTok{\# This computes a matrix of distances between every pre{-}event embedding and every post{-}event embedding.}
\NormalTok{distance\_matrix }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ pairwise\_distances(pre\_event\_embeddings, post\_event\_embeddings)}
\CommentTok{\# We\textquotesingle{}re quantifying how much the embedding space has changed, assuming that semantic changes (like flooding) shift embeddings.}

\CommentTok{\# Get the average of the distance matrix}
\NormalTok{avg\_distance }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ np.mean(distance\_matrix)}
\BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(}\SpecialStringTok{f"Average embedding distance between pre{-} and post{-}event: }\SpecialCharTok{\{}\NormalTok{avg\_distance}\SpecialCharTok{:.4f\}}\SpecialStringTok{"}\NormalTok{)}
\CommentTok{\# One number representing how different the pre/post embeddings are overall.}

\CommentTok{\# Visualize the differences}
\NormalTok{plt.imshow(distance\_matrix, cmap}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"viridis"}\NormalTok{, aspect}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"auto"}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{plt.colorbar(label}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"Embedding Distance"}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{plt.title(}\StringTok{"Pre{-} vs. Post{-}Event Embedding Distances"}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{plt.xlabel(}\StringTok{"Post{-}Event Embeddings"}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{plt.ylabel(}\StringTok{"Pre{-}Event Embeddings"}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{plt.show()}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

It might be helpful to look once more at the images chronologically
ordered after visualizing the graph above.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{stack.sel(band}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{[}\StringTok{"red"}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{"green"}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{"blue"}\NormalTok{]).plot.imshow(}
\NormalTok{    row}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"time"}\NormalTok{, rgb}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"band"}\NormalTok{, vmin}\OperatorTok{=}\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{, vmax}\OperatorTok{=}\DecValTok{25000}\NormalTok{, col\_wrap}\OperatorTok{=}\DecValTok{6}
\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\subsection{Discussion}\label{discussion}

\begin{center}\rule{0.5\linewidth}{0.5pt}\end{center}

\subsubsection{How do I know when EOFMs are the right
tool?}\label{how-do-i-know-when-eofms-are-the-right-tool}

EOFMs are particularly suitable when:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  You have \textbf{limited labeled data}
\item
  Your task requires \textbf{generalizable spatial or temporal
  reasoning}
\item
  You want to \textbf{transfer models across regions or sensors}
\item
  You seek to build \textbf{multi-task pipelines} (e.g., classification
  + segmentation + anomaly detection)
\end{itemize}

They are less appropriate when:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  You have abundant labels and simple classification tasks
\item
  Computational cost is a constraint
\item
  Model interpretability is the top priority
\end{itemize}

\begin{center}\rule{0.5\linewidth}{0.5pt}\end{center}

\subsubsection{How does the use of EOFMs compare to the performance of
other
approaches?}\label{how-does-the-use-of-eofms-compare-to-the-performance-of-other-approaches}

\paragraph{Zero-shot}\label{zero-shot}

This means of use is addressed in the chapter. Zero-shot performance
(without task-specific training) gives a baseline for how well the
pretrained EOFM has generalized from pretraining to your task domain.
This is a strength of Prithvi and CLAY in particular, as they can encode
rich geospatial patterns directly from input data.

\paragraph{Fine-tuning}\label{fine-tuning}

Fine-tuning should be benchmarked alongside zero-shot usage in practice
when using a specialized dataset, or when training for downstream tasks.
Fine-tuning can enable domain-specific adaptation and often improves
accuracy, particularly in edge cases or for features not well
represented in pretraining. This comes with increased computational cost
and overfitting risk if data is limited.

\paragraph{Simpler methods}\label{simpler-methods}

It's essential to benchmark EOFMs against simpler baselines like:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Classical remote sensing indices + Random Forest
\item
  SVM
\item
  Spectral-only MLPs
\item
  Convolutional Neural Networks
\end{itemize}

These comparisons help quantify whether the added complexity of a
foundation model is justified for your task.

\paragraph{Other feature reduction
techniques}\label{other-feature-reduction-techniques}

Other unsupervised methods like pure PCA, autoencoders, or temporal
summaries may be compared to EOFM embeddings when used as input to
downstream classifiers. This allows us to assess whether EOFMs truly
encode more discriminative or transferable features than conventional
feature engineering pipelines.

\begin{center}\rule{0.5\linewidth}{0.5pt}\end{center}

\subsubsection{How does the performance of different models compare
relative to each approach's resource
consumption?}\label{how-does-the-performance-of-different-models-compare-relative-to-each-approachs-resource-consumption}

\begin{longtable}[]{@{}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 8\tabcolsep) * \real{0.2035}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 8\tabcolsep) * \real{0.2212}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 8\tabcolsep) * \real{0.1062}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 8\tabcolsep) * \real{0.1770}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 8\tabcolsep) * \real{0.2920}}@{}}
\toprule\noalign{}
\begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Model Type
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Performance
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Compute Cost
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Training Data Needed
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Suitability
\end{minipage} \\
\midrule\noalign{}
\endhead
\bottomrule\noalign{}
\endlastfoot
EOFM (Prithvi/CLAY) & High (esp.~zero/few shot) & High & Low--Moderate &
Complex, generalizable tasks \\
Random Forest + Indices & Medium & Low & Moderate & Simple tasks \\
CNN from scratch & Medium--High & Medium--High & High & Supervised tasks
with many labels \\
PCA + SVM & Low--Medium & Very Low & Moderate & Baselines, small-scale
tasks \\
\end{longtable}

\begin{center}\rule{0.5\linewidth}{0.5pt}\end{center}

\subsubsection{What kinds of interpretability can be
done?}\label{what-kinds-of-interpretability-can-be-done}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \textbf{Attention maps} (e.g., from ViT-based EOFMs like Prithvi and
  Clay)
\item
  \textbf{Similarity search} using embedding distances (semantic
  matching)
\item
  \textbf{Visualization of embedding spaces} (UMAP/t-SNE for EO tasks)
\end{itemize}

For Prithvi and CLAY, embedding-based analysis (e.g., clustering,
retrieval, similarity) is particularly powerful, though attribution
methods are still emerging.

\begin{center}\rule{0.5\linewidth}{0.5pt}\end{center}

\subsubsection{How can I use the information above to ensure I choose
the right
method?}\label{how-can-i-use-the-information-above-to-ensure-i-choose-the-right-method}

Choosing between methods depends on:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \textbf{Label availability} (EOFMs are strong when labels are scarce)
\item
  \textbf{Task complexity} (fine-grained or abstract tasks benefit more
  from EOFMs)
\item
  \textbf{Computation available} (EOFMs are heavier; classical methods
  are leaner)
\item
  \textbf{Performance delta} (if EOFMs outperform simple methods only
  marginally, simpler methods may be preferable)
\end{itemize}

\begin{center}\rule{0.5\linewidth}{0.5pt}\end{center}

\subsubsection{How can we stratify train, test, and validation to ensure
proper
evaluation?}\label{how-can-we-stratify-train-test-and-validation-to-ensure-proper-evaluation}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \textbf{Spatial stratification}: sample by region or tile, carefully
  to avoid spatial leakage
\item
  \textbf{Temporal stratification}: ensure held-out time stamps in test
  set
\item
  \textbf{Environmental conditions}: vary climate, terrain, and
  seasonality
\item
  \textbf{Stratified sampling}: ensure class balance in all sets
  (important for rare class detection)
\end{itemize}

Avoid random pixel-based splits---these can overestimate performance due
to spatial autocorrelation.

\begin{center}\rule{0.5\linewidth}{0.5pt}\end{center}

\subsubsection{How can we evaluate at both the tile and map level to
ensure proper continuity and qualitative ``reasonability'' of the
output?}\label{how-can-we-evaluate-at-both-the-tile-and-map-level-to-ensure-proper-continuity-and-qualitative-reasonability-of-the-output}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \textbf{Tile-level metrics}: per-tile accuracy, F1, confusion matrices
\item
  \textbf{Map-level coherence}: visually inspect maps for spatial
  discontinuities, fragmentation, or unreasonable transitions
\item
  \textbf{Temporal consistency}: ensure predictions are temporally
  smooth where expected (e.g., gradual seasonal change)
\end{itemize}

Use moving window accuracy checks and expert review of map artifacts.
EOFMs can occasionally ``hallucinate'' features if the embedding has
been poorly grounded.

\begin{center}\rule{0.5\linewidth}{0.5pt}\end{center}

\subsubsection{What to do when one's label set is
limited?}\label{what-to-do-when-ones-label-set-is-limited}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \textbf{Use EOFM embeddings as features}: they encode high-level
  patterns without requiring supervised training
\item
  \textbf{Leverage weak supervision}: use coarsely labeled data
\item
  \textbf{Few-shot fine-tuning}: tune only the last layer with a handful
  of labels
\item
  \textbf{Self-training or pseudo-labeling}: train on confident
  predictions
\item
  \textbf{Domain adaptation}: try transferring from well-labeled regions
  to unlabeled ones.
\end{itemize}

\subsection{Conclusion}\label{conclusion}

In this notebook, we demonstrated how to evaluate two Earth Observation
Foundation Models---Prithvi and Clay---on the 2022 Pakistan floods, one
of the most catastrophic flood events in recent history. Through these
examples, we illustrated how EOFMs can generate meaningful embeddings
from satellite imagery that capture distinct hydrological states:
pre-flood land conditions, active flooding with inundated lands and
turbid water, and post-flood recovery patterns.

The key insight is that these embeddings---learned representations of
the imagery---encode rich semantic information without requiring
manually labeled training data. In traditional flood mapping workflows,
analysts must either manually delineate flood boundaries or train
supervised classifiers on laboriously labeled datasets for each new
event. EOFMs fundamentally change this pattern: because the models have
already learned general-purpose representations of Earth's surface from
vast amounts of satellite data, they can produce embeddings that
distinguish flooded from non-flooded areas. As we showed, a simple
classifier (like an SVM) trained on just a handful of labeled embeddings
can achieve strong flood detection performance. This means disaster
response teams can go from raw satellite imagery to preliminary flood
maps in hours rather than days---critical time savings when lives and
livelihoods are at stake.

For practitioners in flood monitoring and water resources management,
this workflow offers a practical template: acquire imagery, generate
embeddings with a pre-trained EOFM, and apply lightweight classification
or clustering to produce actionable maps. As these foundation models
continue to mature, their integration into operational flood forecasting
and damage assessment systems will become increasingly valuable for
climate adaptation efforts worldwide.

\subsubsection{Next Steps}\label{next-steps}

To build on the techniques demonstrated in this notebook, consider
exploring:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \textbf{Fine-tuning for flood segmentation:} Instead of using
  embeddings with a simple classifier, fine-tune the model with a
  segmentation head to produce pixel-level flood masks.
\item
  \textbf{Multi-temporal change detection:} Leverage the temporal
  capabilities of models like Prithvi to detect flood onset and
  recession dynamics across longer time series.
\item
  \textbf{Transfer to other disasters:} Apply the same embedding-based
  workflow to other natural hazards such as wildfires, landslides, or
  drought monitoring.
\item
  \textbf{Operational integration:} Explore how to integrate EOFM-based
  flood mapping into existing disaster response pipelines, including
  near-real-time satellite data feeds.
\item
  \textbf{Uncertainty quantification:} Investigate methods to estimate
  confidence in flood predictions, which is critical for decision-making
  in humanitarian response.
\end{itemize}
